Java 8 & 17 Features

Lambda expressions, Stream API optimization, lazy evaluation lifecycle, CompletableFuture pipelines, and modern Java (Java 11-17) features like Records, Sealed Classes, and Pattern Matching.

What is the difference between Future and CompletableFuture in Java? How do you compose them?

Future: Introduced in Java 5, Future represents the result of an asynchronous computation. However, it has no callback capabilities; you must block the executing thread by calling get() or poll in a loop using isDone() to retrieve results.

CompletableFuture: Introduced in Java 8, CompletableFuture implements Future and CompletionStage. It provides a rich set of non-blocking monadic methods, enabling you to build asynchronous pipelines with callbacks and reactive mappings.

Pipeline Composition: You can chain asynchronous tasks using thenApply (transform value), thenCompose (flatmap another future), and thenCombine (join two independent futures). It also integrates exception handlers like exceptionally to resolve pipeline failures gracefully.

Thread Management: If no custom executor is supplied, actions run in the common ForkJoinPool thread context. CompletableFuture methods ending in "Async" allow offloading tasks to specific thread pools to prevent blocking core app resources.

CompletableFuturePipeline.java 
import java.util.concurrent.CompletableFuture;
import java.util.concurrent.ExecutorService;
import java.util.concurrent.Executors;

public class CompletableFuturePipeline {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        ExecutorService customPool = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(4);

        // Chain async operations with exception handling and timeouts
        CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> {
            System.out.println("Fetching user on: " + Thread.currentThread().getName());
            return "User-99";
        }, customPool)
        .thenApplyAsync(userId -> {
            System.out.println("Processing profile on: " + Thread.currentThread().getName());
            return userId + " (Senior Developer)";
        }, customPool)
        .thenCompose(profile -> CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> profile + " | Score: 98"))
        .exceptionally(ex -> {
            System.err.println("Pipeline failed: " + ex.getMessage());
            return "Fallback Profile";
        })
        .thenAccept(result -> System.out.println("Final Result: " + result));

        // Sleep main thread briefly to allow async pipeline execution
        Thread.sleep(1000);
        customPool.shutdown();
    }
}

Explain the transition from javax to jakarta package namespace in modern Enterprise Java. What are the key implications?

Migration History: Oracle transferred control of Java EE to the Eclipse Foundation in 2017. As part of this transition, Oracle retained ownership of the "java" and "javax" trademarks, meaning the Eclipse Foundation was forbidden from modifying APIs under the javax.* namespace.

Namespace Change: To evolve the specifications, Eclipse rebranded Java EE to Jakarta EE. Starting with Jakarta EE 9, all enterprise APIs transitioned from the javax.* package namespace to the jakarta.* namespace (e.g., servlet, JPA, validation).

Key Implications: Modern frameworks (Spring Boot 3.x, Hibernate 6.x) depend on Jakarta EE 10. Existing applications migrating to these versions must update all imports (e.g., from javax.persistence.* to jakarta.persistence.*) or use build tool transformation plugins.

Compatibility: Legacy libraries using javax.* will crash at runtime with NoClassDefFoundError in modern application servers (like Tomcat 10+ or WildFly 27+) unless an active class bytecode transformation layer is configured.

NamespaceMigrationDemo.java 
// OLD WAY (Java EE / Spring Boot 2.x)
// import javax.persistence.Entity;
// import javax.persistence.Id;
// import javax.persistence.Table;
// import javax.servlet.http.HttpServletRequest;

// NEW WAY (Jakarta EE / Spring Boot 3.x)
import jakarta.persistence.Entity;
import jakarta.persistence.Id;
import jakarta.persistence.Table;
import jakarta.persistence.GeneratedValue;
import jakarta.persistence.GenerationType;

@Entity
@Table(name = "users")
public class NamespaceMigrationDemo {
    @Id
    @GeneratedValue(strategy = GenerationType.IDENTITY)
    private Long id;
    
    private String username;

    public NamespaceMigrationDemo() {}

    public Long getId() { return id; }
    public String getUsername() { return username; }
}

What is a Lambda Expression in Java? Compare general Functional Interfaces with the java.util.function.Predicate interface.

Lambda Expression: A lambda expression is a short block of code that accepts parameters and returns a value. It provides a clean, expressive way to represent a functional interface inline without creating an anonymous class boilerplate, improving readability in stream operations.

Functional Interface: A functional interface is any interface that contains exactly one abstract method (Single Abstract Method - SAM). It can contain multiple default or static methods, and is marked with the optional @FunctionalInterface annotation to enforce compilation checks.

Predicate Interface: Predicate<T> is a built-in functional interface defined in java.util.function. It declares a single abstract method boolean test(T t), designed to evaluate an input object against a condition and return a boolean.

Comparison: While a general functional interface can return any type or throw custom checked exceptions, Predicate is strictly a boolean-valued function. Predicates can also be chained together using default methods like and(), or(), and negate().

LambdaPredicateDemo.java 
import java.util.List;
import java.util.function.Predicate;
import java.util.stream.Collectors;

public class LambdaPredicateDemo {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        List<String> frameworkList = List.of("Spring", "React", "Kafka", "Hibernate", "Angular");

        // 1. Defining a Predicate using a Lambda expression
        Predicate<String> startWithS = str -> str.startsWith("S");
        Predicate<String> longName = str -> str.length() > 5;

        // 2. Chaining Predicates using default methods
        Predicate<String> combinedCondition = startWithS.or(longName);

        List<String> filteredList = frameworkList.stream()
            .filter(combinedCondition)
            .collect(Collectors.toList());

        System.out.println("Filtered: " + filteredList);

        // 3. Custom Functional Interface usage
        Formatter uppercaseFormatter = text -> text.toUpperCase();
        System.out.println("Custom Format: " + uppercaseFormatter.format("valorepos"));
    }
}

@FunctionalInterface
interface Formatter {
    String format(String input);
}

Explain the usage of static, final, and default keywords in Java. What is the significance of the main method's signature?

Static: The static keyword indicates that a member (variable, method, or nested class) belongs to the class itself rather than to instances of the class. Static members are initialized once when the class is loaded into memory, shared across all instances, and can be accessed without creating an object.

Final: The final keyword enforces immutability and restricts inheritance. (1) A final variable cannot be reassigned once initialized. (2) A final method cannot be overridden by subclasses. (3) A final class cannot be subclassed, which is crucial for creating immutable classes like String.

Default: Introduced in Java 8, default methods allow interfaces to declare method implementations. This enables developers to add new methods to existing interfaces without breaking the classes that implement them, maintaining backward compatibility.

Main Method Signature: The standard entry point public static void main(String[] args) is designed for JVM startup: public permits the JVM to invoke it from outside the package, static allows invocation without instantiating the class, void specifies that it returns no value, and String[] args accepts runtime command-line arguments.

StaticFinalDefaultDemo.java 
public class StaticFinalDefaultDemo {
    // Shared class-level constant
    public static final String PLATFORM_NAME = "Enterprise-Core";
    
    // Instance-level constant
    private final int instanceId;

    public StaticFinalDefaultDemo(int id) {
        this.instanceId = id; // Must be initialized in constructor
    }

    public static void printPlatform() {
        System.out.println("Platform: " + PLATFORM_NAME);
    }

    public static void main(String[] args) {
        StaticFinalDefaultDemo.printPlatform();
        StaticFinalDefaultDemo demo = new StaticFinalDefaultDemo(101);
        System.out.println("Instance ID: " + demo.instanceId);
        
        // Invoke interface default method
        CustomService service = new CustomServiceImpl();
        service.executeTask();
    }
}

interface CustomService {
    // Default method providing implementation
    default void executeTask() {
        System.out.println("Default execution log...");
    }
}

class CustomServiceImpl implements CustomService {
    // Class implements interface, inherits default method
}

What are the major features introduced in Java 17? Explain Records, Sealed Classes, Pattern Matching, and Switch Expressions.

Java 17 (released in September 2021) is a Long-Term Support (LTS) release containing several major language improvements:

  • Records: Concise immutable data carriers. The compiler automatically generates the constructor, getters (without `get` prefix), `equals()`, `hashCode()`, and `toString()`. Fields are final and records cannot extend other classes.
  • Sealed Classes & Interfaces: Allow class developers to restrict which classes/interfaces can extend or implement them. Useful for domain modeling where hierarchies are closed and known. Subclasses must be declared as `final`, `sealed`, or `non-sealed`.
  • Pattern Matching for instanceof: Eliminates the boilerplate of explicit casting. You declare the binding variable directly in the `instanceof` check.
  • Switch Expressions: Return values directly from switch blocks, use arrow syntax (`->`), and guarantee compile-time exhaustiveness (especially when matching sealed hierarchies).

Java17FeaturesDemo.java 
// 1. Records
public record UserDto(String username, String email) {}

// 2. Sealed Classes
public sealed interface Shape permits Circle, Rectangle {}

public final class Circle implements Shape {
    private final double radius;
    public Circle(double radius) { this.radius = radius; }
    public double radius() { return radius; }
}

public final class Rectangle implements Shape {
    private final double length, width;
    public Rectangle(double l, double w) { this.length = l; this.width = w; }
    public double getArea() { return length * width; }
}

public class Java17FeaturesDemo {
    // 3. Switch Expressions & Pattern Matching
    public static double getMetrics(Shape shape) {
        return switch (shape) {
            case Circle c -> Math.PI * c.radius() * c.radius(); // Pattern matching bind
            case Rectangle r -> r.getArea();
        }; // Switch expression returns value and ensures exhaustiveness
    }

    public static void main(String[] args) {
        UserDto user = new UserDto("biratsaha", "birat@website.com");
        System.out.println("Record Auto-ToString: " + user);
        
        Shape circle = new Circle(5.0);
        System.out.println("Circle Area via Switch Expression: " + getMetrics(circle));
    }
}

How do you design non-blocking asynchronous pipelines using CompletableFuture? Show how to handle pipeline errors and enforce timeouts.

CompletableFuture (introduced in Java 8) implements the Future and CompletionStage interfaces, enabling non-blocking declarative task chaining. Unlike classic Futures, it supports callbacks (e.g., thenApply, thenAccept, thenCompose) that execute asynchronously when upstream computations complete, avoiding thread blocking (`.get()`).

Pipeline Error Handling: Exceptions that occur in asynchronous stages propagate down the pipeline. You can intercept and recover from exceptions using:

  • exceptionally(): Acts as a catch-block; returns a default fallback value of the same type.
  • handle(): Receives both the result and the exception, allowing you to transform either into a new output.
  • whenComplete(): Serves as a finally-block, executing callbacks without altering the pipeline value.

Timeout Enforcement (Java 9+): You can use `.orTimeout(timeout, unit)` to throw a TimeoutException if a stage does not complete within limits, or `.completeOnTimeout(defaultValue, timeout, unit)` to return a default value on timeout.

AsyncOrchestrator.java 
import java.util.concurrent.CompletableFuture;
import java.util.concurrent.TimeUnit;

public class AsyncOrchestrator {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> {
            // Simulate fetching user details from remote microservice
            sleep(100);
            return "User: Birat";
        })
        .thenCompose(user -> CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> {
            // Fetch configuration/roles based on user details
            sleep(150);
            return user + " | Role: Admin";
        }))
        // Enforce a strict timeout of 500ms on the pipeline
        .orTimeout(500, TimeUnit.MILLISECONDS)
        // Handle success/failure smoothly
        .handle((result, ex) -> {
            if (ex != null) {
                System.err.println("Pipeline failed: " + ex.getMessage());
                return "Fallback User Config (Default)";
            }
            return result;
        })
        .thenAccept(finalResult -> System.out.println("Result: " + finalResult))
        .join(); // Block main thread to allow async threads to finish output
    }

    private static void sleep(long ms) {
        try { Thread.sleep(ms); } catch (InterruptedException e) { Thread.currentThread().interrupt(); }
    }
}

Explain the evaluation lifecycle, performance, and memory implications of Java Streams vs standard loops. When do parallel streams degrade throughput?

Evaluation Lifecycle: Java Streams are built on a pipeline architecture comprised of a source, zero or more intermediate operations, and exactly one terminal operation. Operations are divided into lazy (e.g., filter, map, flatMap) and eager (terminal operations like collect, findFirst, forEach). Lazy operations do not perform any processing until the terminal operation is executed. During execution, intermediate operations are fused together in a single pass over the elements (loop fusion), minimizing traversal overhead.

Architectural Tip: ForkJoinPool Sharing

By default, all parallel streams execute using the JVM-wide common ForkJoinPool (ForkJoinPool.commonPool()). If a parallel stream performs blocking I/O (e.g., external REST API calls or DB queries), it will saturate the common pool, blocking threads across the entire application. To safeguard production systems, wrap blocking stream operations inside a custom, isolated ForkJoinPool context.

Memory & Performance: Streams incur Object Allocation overhead due to pipeline wrapper instantiation, lambda context capture, and primitive boxing. If processing million-record collections of primitives (like integers), using Stream<Integer> creates massive boxing overhead compared to primitive loops. Use specialized primitive streams like IntStream, LongStream, or DoubleStream to eliminate boxing.

Parallel Streams Degrade Performance When:
  • Small Data Sizes: The overhead of partitioning the collection, context-switching worker threads, and merging sub-results exceeds the cost of single-threaded linear iterations.
  • Splitting Costs are High: Streams backed by LinkedList split terribly (O(N) partition time) compared to arrays or ArrayList (O(1) split time).
  • Stateful Operations: Operators like sorted(), distinct(), or limit() require global coordination, rendering parallelization highly inefficient.
CustomForkJoinParallelStream.java 
import java.util.List;
import java.util.concurrent.ForkJoinPool;
import java.util.stream.Collectors;
import java.util.stream.IntStream;

public class CustomForkJoinParallelStream {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        List<Integer> dataset = IntStream.range(1, 100000).boxed().collect(Collectors.toList());

        // Create an isolated ForkJoinPool with custom parallelism
        ForkJoinPool customPool = new ForkJoinPool(4); 
        
        try {
            // Executing stream execution inside custom pool thread context
            List<Double> processed = customPool.submit(() -> 
                dataset.parallelStream()
                       .filter(n -> n % 2 == 0)
                       .map(Math::sqrt)
                       .collect(Collectors.toList())
            ).get(); // Synchronous block for execution
            
            System.out.println("Processed size: " + processed.size());
        } finally {
            customPool.shutdown(); // Ensure resource cleanup
        }
    }
}

What is a functional interface?

A Functional Interface is an interface that has exactly one abstract method (SAM - Single Abstract Method). It can contain any number of default or static methods. It is marked with the optional `@FunctionalInterface` annotation, which causes the compiler to verify its structure.

Java 8 provides standard functional interfaces in the java.util.function package:

  • Predicate<T>: Takes one argument of type `T` and returns a `boolean` (method: `test(T)`).
  • Function<T, R>: Takes one argument of type `T` and returns a result of type `R` (method: `apply(T)`).
  • Consumer<T>: Takes one argument of type `T` and returns `void` (method: `accept(T)`).
  • Supplier<T>: Takes no arguments and returns a result of type `T` (method: `get()`).

Optional Usage: Optional<T> is a container object used to represent the presence or absence of a value, helping prevent NullPointerException. Best practices: Avoid using Optional as field variables or method parameter types (intended solely for method return types). Use functional pipelines like `map()`, `filter()`, and `flatMap()`, or fallback operations like `orElse()` and `orElseGet()`, instead of nesting `if (opt.isPresent())` blocks.

FunctionalInterfaceAndOptional.java 
import java.util.Optional;
import java.util.function.Function;
import java.util.function.Predicate;

public class FunctionalInterfaceAndOptional {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        Predicate<String> isNumeric = str -> str != null && str.matches("\\d+");
        Function<String, Integer> toInt = Integer::parseInt;

        String rawInput = "1024";

        // Optional pipeline mapping and filtering
        Optional<Integer> result = Optional.ofNullable(rawInput)
            .filter(isNumeric)
            .map(toInt);

        // Print value if present, otherwise fallback
        System.out.println("Mapped Integer: " + result.orElse(-1));
    }
}

What are method references?

Core Answer: In response to the query about method references, A shorthand for calling methods using :: syntax. E.g., System.out::println.

Production Standards: For Spring Boot modules utilizing MethodReferencesService, enforce proper layering (Controller, Service, Repository), leverage dependency injection (IoC), and separate profiles configuration.

Observability & Monitoring: Track bean creation steps, request handler latencies, and transaction boundary status for MethodReferencesService using Spring Boot Actuator and SLF4J logs.

Performance Guidelines: Streamline resource consumption for MethodReferencesService by utilizing lazy bean loading, minimizing database connection times, and configuring connection pool limits.

What is Optional and how should it be used?

Core Answer: In response to the query about optional and how should it be used, To avoid null. Use isPresent(), orElse(), map(). Don’t misuse as container.

Production Standards: For Spring Boot modules utilizing OptionalUsage, enforce proper layering (Controller, Service, Repository), leverage dependency injection (IoC), and separate profiles configuration.

Observability & Monitoring: Track bean creation steps, request handler latencies, and transaction boundary status for OptionalUsage using Spring Boot Actuator and SLF4J logs.

Performance Guidelines: Streamline resource consumption for OptionalUsage by utilizing lazy bean loading, minimizing database connection times, and configuring connection pool limits.

OptionalUsage.java 
import java.util.Optional;

public class OptionalUsage {
    public String resolveUserEmail(UserRecord user) {
        // Optional avoids NullPointerExceptions via declarative mapping
        return Optional.ofNullable(user)
                       .map(UserRecord::getEmail)
                       .orElse("default@valore.com");
    }
}
class UserRecord { public String getEmail() { return "birat@valore.com"; } }

Runtime & Concurrency

JVM & Multithreading

Garbage collection (G1GC vs ZGC), JVM memory spaces, deadlocks detection and prevention, thread safety, synchronized vs ReentrantLock, volatile, ThreadLocal, static vs final, and sorting utilities.

How many threads can a JVM create? What configuration parameters and system limits restrict thread allocation?

Thread Allocation Limits: The maximum thread limit is not a fixed JVM setting; it is determined by OS limitations and available hardware resources.

Operating System Limits: Key constraints include: (1) OS maximum user processes (ulimit -u), (2) kernel max threads (/proc/sys/kernel/threads-max), and (3) available physical RAM.

Stack Memory Constraint: Each thread reserves a thread stack size (specified via -Xss, usually 1MB). If you allocate too many threads, physical RAM gets depleted.

Out of Memory: Exhausting these limits throws java.lang.OutOfMemoryError: unable to create new native thread. Use bounded ThreadPools to prevent crash-on-spike.

BoundedThreadPoolConfig.java 
import java.util.concurrent.*;

public class BoundedThreadPoolConfig {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        // Enforce thread boundaries to prevent native memory exhaustion
        int corePoolSize = 10;
        int maxPoolSize = 50;
        long keepAliveTime = 60L;
        
        // Use a bounded queue to prevent Infinite Memory Accumulation (OOM)
        BlockingQueue<Runnable> queue = new LinkedBlockingQueue<>(500);

        // Custom ThreadFactory to name threads for debugging
        ThreadFactory threadFactory = new ThreadFactory() {
            private int count = 1;
            @Override
            public Thread newThread(Runnable r) {
                return new Thread(r, "AppWorker-" + count++);
            }
        };

        // AbortPolicy rejects tasks when thread limits and queue boundaries are saturated
        RejectedExecutionHandler handler = new ThreadPoolExecutor.AbortPolicy();

        ExecutorService executor = new ThreadPoolExecutor(
            corePoolSize,
            maxPoolSize,
            keepAliveTime,
            TimeUnit.SECONDS,
            queue,
            threadFactory,
            handler
        );

        System.out.println("Bounded thread pool initialized safely.");
        executor.shutdown();
    }
}

How does Garbage Collection reclaim memory in the JVM? Compare G1GC vs ZGC collectors and their production configuration.

Generational Heap: JVM Garbage Collection organizes the heap into Young (Eden, Survivor spaces) and Old generations. Objects are created in Eden, promoted to survivor spaces, and eventually moved to the Old generation if they persist.

G1GC (Garbage-First): Designed for multi-gigabyte heaps, G1GC divides the heap into thousands of virtual regions. It prioritizes regions containing the most garbage, compacting space concurrently while tracking a configurable target pause time.

ZGC (Z Garbage Collector): A low-latency, scalable collector. ZGC performs memory reclamation concurrently with application threads, using colored pointers and load barriers to resolve memory state issues.

GC Pause Times: ZGC restricts pause times to under a millisecond regardless of heap size, making it suitable for modern enterprise microservices running on huge heaps (GBs to TBs).

GcPropertiesConfig.sh 
#!/bin/bash
# Production JVM Flags for Garbage Collection tuning

# 1. Enable G1GC (Default in Java 9+) with custom target pause time
java -XX:+UseG1GC -XX:MaxGCPauseMillis=200 -jar microservice-app.jar

# 2. Enable ZGC (Low-latency concurrent collector available in Java 15+)
# Suitable for containerized environments requiring predictable, sub-millisecond pauses
java -XX:+UseZGC -XX:+UnlockDiagnosticVMOptions -XX:+UseNUMA -jar high-throughput-app.jar

# Useful monitoring flags
# java -Xlog:gc*:file=gc.log:time,uptime:filecount=5,filesize=100M -jar app.jar

Compare Heap vs Stack memory in Java. Where are objects and class definitions saved? Discuss primitive vs wrapper classes.

Stack Memory: Stack memory stores thread execution frames, local primitive variables, and references to objects on the heap. It is allocated per thread, operates in LIFO order, is extremely fast, and requires no garbage collection.

Heap Memory: Heap memory stores all instantiated objects and arrays. It is shared across all application threads, has a dynamic size, and requires Garbage Collection (GC) overhead to reclaim memory.

Metaspace: Introduced in Java 8 to replace PermGen, Metaspace resides in native off-heap memory. It stores class definitions, method metadata, static variables, and the runtime constant pool.

Primitives vs Wrappers: Primitive types (e.g. int) store values directly on the stack (unless they are fields inside a heap-allocated object). Wrapper types (e.g. Integer) are full objects allocated on the heap, introducing boxing and unboxing performance overhead.

MemoryAllocationDemo.java 
public class MemoryAllocationDemo {
    // Stored in Metaspace (metadata and class reference)
    private static final String APP_NAME = "Memory-Demo"; 

    public static void main(String[] args) {
        // Primitive variable value (100) is stored directly in the Stack frame
        int primitiveAge = 35; 
        
        // Wrapper object variable ref (on stack) points to an Integer object on the Heap
        Integer objectAge = Integer.valueOf(35); 
        
        // Custom object reference (on stack) points to a User object on the Heap
        User user = new User("Birat"); 
        
        executeLocalLogic(user);
    } // Stack frame is popped here, but Heap objects await Garbage Collection

    private static void executeLocalLogic(User u) {
        String status = "ACTIVE"; // Reference on Stack pointing to Constant Pool
        System.out.println("Processing: " + u.getName());
    }
}

class User {
    private final String name; // Reference variable pointing to String on Heap
    public User(String name) { this.name = name; }
    public String getName() { return name; }
}

How do hash collisions occur in Java HashMaps? How are they resolved internally in Java 8+?

Hash Collisions: A collision occurs when two distinct keys yield the same hash code and map to the exact same bucket index in the underlying node array. The HashMap must store both entries in the same bucket.

Chaining: Historically, HashMaps resolved collisions using linked lists. Multiple colliding nodes are chained together in a singly linked list structure inside the bucket, degrading lookup performance to O(N).

Treeification: In Java 8+, if a bucket's node count exceeds the threshold TREEIFY_THRESHOLD = 8, and the total table capacity is at least 64, the linked list is converted into a balanced Red-Black Tree.

Time Complexity: Treeification optimizes lookup performance, changing worst-case time complexity from O(N) to O(log N). This protects applications against Hash Collision Denial of Service (DoS) attacks.

HashMapCollisionDemo.java 
import java.util.HashMap;
import java.util.Map;

public class HashMapCollisionDemo {
    
    // Class designed to intentionally generate collisions
    static class CollidingKey {
        private final int id;
        private final String value;

        public CollidingKey(int id, String value) {
            this.id = id;
            this.value = value;
        }

        @Override
        public int hashCode() {
            return 42; // Force every key into the exact same bucket
        }

        @Override
        public boolean equals(Object obj) {
            if (this == obj) return true;
            if (!(obj instanceof CollidingKey)) return false;
            return this.id == ((CollidingKey) obj).id;
        }
    }

    public static void main(String[] args) {
        Map<CollidingKey, String> map = new HashMap<>();
        
        // Insert multiple items that share the same hashCode bucket
        for (int i = 0; i < 12; i++) {
            map.put(new CollidingKey(i, "Value-" + i), "Data-" + i);
        }
        
        // In Java 8+, this bucket transitions from Linked List to Red-Black Tree
        System.out.println("Successfully stored colliding keys, size: " + map.size());
    }
}

What makes a collection thread-safe? Compare Fail-Fast vs Fail-Safe iterators with real-world collection examples.

Thread Safety: A collection is thread-safe if it guarantees structural and data consistency under concurrent modifications by multiple threads. This is achieved via mutual exclusion locks, lock-striping, or copy-on-write mechanisms.

Fail-Fast Iterators: Iterators from standard collections (like ArrayList or HashMap) are fail-fast. If the collection is modified structurally during iteration, the iterator immediately throws a ConcurrentModificationException.

ModCount Mechanism: Fail-fast iterators track modification states using a volatile modCount counter. At each step, the iterator checks if the counter has changed; if a mismatch is detected, it triggers a fast-fail crash.

Fail-Safe Iterators: Iterators from concurrent collections (like CopyOnWriteArrayList or ConcurrentHashMap) are fail-safe. They operate on a copy or snapshot of the collection, allowing concurrent updates without raising exceptions.

IteratorComparison.java 
import java.util.ArrayList;
import java.util.List;
import java.util.concurrent.CopyOnWriteArrayList;
import java.util.Iterator;

public class IteratorComparison {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        // Fail-Fast Example
        List<String> list1 = new ArrayList<>();
        list1.add("Java"); list1.add("Spring");
        
        try {
            Iterator<String> it = list1.iterator();
            while(it.hasNext()) {
                String val = it.next();
                list1.add("New-Tech"); // Will trigger ConcurrentModificationException on next iteration
            }
        } catch (Exception e) {
            System.out.println("Fail-Fast detected: " + e.toString());
        }

        // Fail-Safe / Fail-Safe Example
        List<String> list2 = new CopyOnWriteArrayList<>();
        list2.add("React"); list2.add("Node");

        Iterator<String> it2 = list2.iterator();
        while(it2.hasNext()) {
            String val = it2.next();
            list2.add("Kubernetes"); // Safe! Modifying copy, iterator holds snapshot
        }
        System.out.println("Fail-Safe final list size: " + list2.size());
    }
}

Compare ArrayList, LinkedList, and Vector in Java. How do they perform under different memory and concurrency workloads?

ArrayList: Backed by a dynamically resizing array. It provides O(1) time complexity for random access index lookup. However, inserting or deleting elements from arbitrary positions requires O(N) time because subsequent elements must be shifted in memory.

LinkedList: Structured as a doubly-linked list. It provides O(1) insertions or deletions at endpoints. Random access, however, requires O(N) traversal from the head or tail to find the target index, and it consumes more memory per node to store address pointers.

Vector: A legacy, thread-safe dynamic array. It synchronizes every operation using monitor locks. This ensures thread safety but introduces significant synchronization overhead, making it inefficient for single-threaded or modern concurrent pipelines.

Selection Strategy: Use ArrayList as the default list. Switch to LinkedList only if your workload relies heavily on head/tail insertions (like queues or deques). Under concurrent conditions, use a lock-free CopyOnWriteArrayList or wrap lists using Collections.synchronizedList.

CollectionBenchmarks.java 
import java.util.ArrayList;
import java.util.LinkedList;
import java.util.List;
import java.util.Vector;

public class CollectionBenchmarks {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        int capacity = 1_000_000;
        
        // 1. Benchmark ArrayList Random Access
        List<Integer> arrayList = new ArrayList<>(capacity);
        for(int i = 0; i < capacity; i++) arrayList.add(i);
        long start = System.nanoTime();
        int val1 = arrayList.get(capacity / 2);
        long arrayTime = System.nanoTime() - start;

        // 2. Benchmark LinkedList Random Access
        List<Integer> linkedList = new LinkedList<>();
        for(int i = 0; i < capacity; i++) linkedList.add(i);
        start = System.nanoTime();
        int val2 = linkedList.get(capacity / 2);
        long linkedTime = System.nanoTime() - start;

        System.out.println("ArrayList O(1) Get Time: " + arrayTime + " ns");
        System.out.println("LinkedList O(N) Get Time: " + linkedTime + " ns");
    }
}

Compare Comparable vs Comparator interfaces. When should each be used?

Comparable Interface: The Comparable interface defines the natural ordering of objects. It requires implementing the compareTo(T o) method directly within the target class, which can only support a single, default sorting strategy.

Comparator Interface: The Comparator interface represents an external, pluggable sorting logic. It implements the compare(T o1, T o2) method, allowing you to define multiple independent sorting strategies for the same class.

Use Cases: Use Comparable to establish a default sorting behavior (e.g. sorting Employee objects by ID). Use Comparator when you need to sort by alternate criteria (e.g. by salary, name, or hire date) or when you cannot modify the source code of the target class.

Modern API Support: Java 8 enhanced Comparator with static helper methods like comparing() and thenComparing(), which integrate cleanly with lambda syntax to build complex sorting chains.

SortingComparisons.java 
import java.util.ArrayList;
import java.util.Collections;
import java.util.Comparator;
import java.util.List;

class Developer implements Comparable<Developer> {
    String name;
    int yoe; // Years of Experience

    public Developer(String name, int yoe) {
        this.name = name;
        this.yoe = yoe;
    }

    // Default Natural Sorting: Sorted by YOE ascending
    @Override
    public int compareTo(Developer other) {
        return Integer.compare(this.yoe, other.yoe);
    }

    @Override
    public String toString() {
        return name + " (" + yoe + " YOE)";
    }
}

public class SortingComparisons {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        List<Developer> team = new ArrayList<>();
        team.add(new Developer("Birat", 8));
        team.add(new Developer("Amit", 5));
        team.add(new Developer("Roy", 12));

        // 1. Natural Sort using Comparable (Ascending YOE)
        Collections.sort(team);
        System.out.println("Natural Sort: " + team);

        // 2. Custom Sort using Comparator Lambda (Sorted by Name)
        team.sort(Comparator.comparing(dev -> dev.name));
        System.out.println("Name Sort: " + team);

        // 3. Reverse Sort using custom Comparator
        team.sort((d1, d2) -> Integer.compare(d2.yoe, d1.yoe));
        System.out.println("Experience Descending: " + team);
    }
}

How do Anonymous Inner Classes work in Java? Show the different approaches to create and run Threads.

Anonymous Inner Classes: An anonymous inner class is an inline, unnamed local class definition that simultaneously declares and instantiates a class. It is ideal for implementing single-use interfaces or extending base classes without polluting the package namespace.

Thread Creation Patterns: Threads can be created in several ways: (1) Extending the Thread class, (2) implementing Runnable, (3) creating anonymous classes, or (4) using modern Lambda expressions.

Resource Overhead: Creating threads using raw constructors like new Thread().start() consumes massive OS resources, as each thread reserves a default thread stack size (typically 1MB) and incurs context-switching overhead.

Enterprise Standards: Production code should avoid raw thread creation. Instead, implement Runnable or Callable and submit tasks to a managed thread pool like ExecutorService for thread recycling.

ThreadInstantiationDemo.java 
import java.util.concurrent.ExecutorService;
import java.util.concurrent.Executors;

public class ThreadInstantiationDemo {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        // Approach 1: Anonymous Inner Class implementing Runnable
        Thread thread1 = new Thread(new Runnable() {
            @Override
            public void run() {
                System.out.println("Anonymous inner class execution");
            }
        });
        
        // Approach 2: Lambda Expression (Java 8+)
        Thread thread2 = new Thread(() -> System.out.println("Lambda thread execution"));

        thread1.start();
        thread2.start();

        // Approach 3: ExecutorService Managed Thread Pool (Enterprise Standard)
        ExecutorService executor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(2);
        executor.submit(() -> System.out.println("Task running inside thread pool"));
        executor.shutdown();
    }
}

What are Marker Interfaces in Java? Discuss how Serialization works, including its security and performance pitfalls.

Marker Interfaces: A marker interface (also called tagged interface) is an interface containing no declared methods or constants (e.g. Serializable, Cloneable). It acts as a metadata tag, instructing the compiler or JVM to apply specific runtime behavior.

Serialization Mechanics: Object serialization converts an object's complete instance state into a portable binary byte stream. This stream can be written to disk, stored in memory, or sent over networks. Deserialization reverses this, reconstructing the object state.

Security Pitfalls: Deserialization is highly vulnerable. If an application deserializes untrusted streams, attackers can inject malicious class states (gadget chains) that trigger remote code execution (RCE) inside the JVM.

Performance Overhead: Default Java serialization is slow and bloats file sizes because it embeds complete class metadata, graph structures, and fully qualified class path descriptors in the byte stream. Modern systems prefer lighter alternatives like JSON or Protocol Buffers.

SecureSerializationDemo.java 
import java.io.*;

public class SecureSerializationDemo implements Serializable {
    private static final long serialVersionUID = 78923L; // Explicit Versioning

    private String userEmail;
    private transient String passwordHash; // Avoid serializing sensitive credentials

    public SecureSerializationDemo(String email, String pwd) {
        this.userEmail = email;
        this.passwordHash = pwd;
    }

    // Custom validation logic during deserialization to mitigate security risks
    private void readObject(ObjectInputStream ois) throws IOException, ClassNotFoundException {
        ois.defaultReadObject(); // Read standard fields
        
        // Enforce validation constraints on incoming data
        if (this.userEmail == null || !this.userEmail.contains("@")) {
            throw new InvalidObjectException("Corrupt or invalid state payload received.");
        }
        System.out.println("Deserialization validated successfully.");
    }
}

What is the volatile keyword in Java? Compare transient vs volatile variables.

Volatile: The volatile keyword prevents threads from caching variables in CPU registers, forcing all reads and writes directly to the shared Main Memory. This guarantees instant visibility of variable state updates across multiple active threads.

Instruction Reordering: Volatile also establishes a happens-before relationship, preventing the JVM compiler and CPU from reordering instructions around reads and writes, which is critical for Double-Checked Locking Singletons.

Transient: The transient keyword is used in the context of object serialization. When an object is serialized, fields marked as transient are ignored, and they assume default null or zero values upon deserialization.

Comparison: Volatile is a concurrency modifier addressing thread visibility and memory coherency during execution, while transient is a persistence modifier addressing field exclusions during byte-stream transformations. They address completely distinct runtime aspects.

VolatileTransientDemo.java 
import java.io.Serializable;

public class VolatileTransientDemo implements Serializable {
    private static final long serialVersionUID = 1L;

    // Transient field - will NOT be serialized
    private transient String temporarySessionToken;
    
    private String username;

    // Volatile variable - ensures thread visibility across memory boundaries
    private transient volatile boolean isWorkerActive = true; 

    public VolatileTransientDemo(String username, String token) {
        this.username = username;
        this.temporarySessionToken = token;
    }

    public void stopWorker() {
        this.isWorkerActive = false; // Writes immediately flush to main memory
    }

    public boolean checkWorkerStatus() {
        return this.isWorkerActive; // Reads directly from main memory
    }
}

Explain volatile, ThreadLocal, static, final, transient, and Comparable vs Comparator with complete Java examples.

These core keywords and interfaces govern thread safety, memory residency, and object behavior:

  • volatile: Guarantees that changes to a variable are visible to all threads immediately. It forces reads and writes to go directly to **main memory** instead of CPU cache, and prevents instruction reordering. However, it does **not** guarantee atomicity (e.g., `count++` is not safe).
  • transient: Prevents fields from being serialized. When writing objects to streams, transient fields are skipped and restored as default values (e.g., null for objects, 0 for integers).
  • ThreadLocal: Provides thread-confined variables. Each thread holds an isolated copy of the variable inside its internal `ThreadLocalMap`. Useful for database connections, session context, or transaction IDs.
  • Comparable vs Comparator: Comparable defines the *natural sorting order* (implemented inside the target class via `compareTo(T)`). Comparator defines *external, custom sorting strategies* (implemented outside the class via `compare(T, T)` or lambda).

KeywordExamples.java 
import java.io.Serializable;
import java.util.ArrayList;
import java.util.Collections;
import java.util.Comparator;
import java.util.List;

public class KeywordExamples implements Serializable {
    private static final long serialVersionUID = 1L;

    private static final ThreadLocal<String> transactionContext = 
        ThreadLocal.withInitial(() -> "TX-Default");

    private static volatile boolean appRunning = true; // Visibility guard
    private transient String sensitiveToken = "SSN-123-X45"; // Never serialized

    public static class Employee implements Comparable<Employee> {
        private final String name;
        private final double salary;

        public Employee(String name, double salary) {
            this.name = name;
            this.salary = salary;
        }

        // Natural sort by Salary ASC
        @Override
        public int compareTo(Employee other) {
            return Double.compare(this.salary, other.salary);
        }

        @Override
        public String toString() { return name + " ($" + salary + ")"; }
    }

    public static void main(String[] args) {
        List<Employee> emps = new ArrayList<>();
        emps.add(new Employee("Birat", 150000));
        emps.add(new Employee("Alex", 120000));

        // Sort via Comparable natural order (Salary)
        Collections.sort(emps);
        System.out.println("Comparable Sort: " + emps);

        // Sort via Comparator (Name order)
        emps.sort((e1, e2) -> e1.name.compareTo(e2.name));
        System.out.println("Comparator Name Sort: " + emps);
        
        // ThreadLocal usage
        transactionContext.set("TX-User-Birat");
        System.out.println("Current Thread Context: " + transactionContext.get());
        transactionContext.remove(); // Clean up thread local to prevent memory leaks
    }
}

What is the difference between synchronized and ReentrantLock? How do you prevent and resolve deadlocks?

synchronized is an intrinsic JVM keyword that provides structured mutual exclusion. The lock is acquired on the object monitor automatically upon entering the block/method and released when exiting (even in exception cases). It cannot be configured for fair locking, nor can it be polled without blocking.

ReentrantLock (from `java.util.concurrent.locks`) is a class-based lock. It must be manually locked and unlocked (within a `try-finally` block). It offers features like:

  • tryLock(): Attempt to acquire the lock immediately or with a timeout. Helps prevent blocking.
  • lockInterruptibly(): Allows waiting threads to respond to interrupts.
  • Fair Locking: Can prioritize threads that have been waiting the longest.

Deadlock Prevention and Resolution:

A deadlock occurs when Thread A holds Lock 1 and waits for Lock 2, while Thread B holds Lock 2 and waits for Lock 1.

  • Lock Ordering: Ensure all threads acquire locks in the exact same order.
  • Lock Timeout: Use `ReentrantLock.tryLock(timeout, unit)` instead of blocking.
  • Diagnostics: Use `jcmd Thread.print` or `jstack ` to extract thread dumps. The JVM automatically identifies deadlocked threads and prints their lock ownership chains.

DeadlockPrevention.java 
import java.util.concurrent.locks.ReentrantLock;
import java.util.concurrent.TimeUnit;

public class DeadlockPrevention {
    private final ReentrantLock lockA = new ReentrantLock();
    private final ReentrantLock lockB = new ReentrantLock();

    public void safeMethod() {
        boolean acquiredA = false;
        boolean acquiredB = false;
        try {
            // Attempt to acquire both locks within 200ms
            acquiredA = lockA.tryLock(200, TimeUnit.MILLISECONDS);
            acquiredB = lockB.tryLock(200, TimeUnit.MILLISECONDS);
            
            if (acquiredA && acquiredB) {
                // Critical section safely executed
                System.out.println("Processing transaction under safe lock ownership.");
            } else {
                System.out.println("Could not acquire locks safely. Backing off to retry...");
            }
        } catch (InterruptedException e) {
            Thread.currentThread().interrupt();
        } finally {
            if (acquiredB) lockB.unlock();
            if (acquiredA) lockA.unlock();
        }
    }
}

Compare the core Garbage Collectors (G1GC vs ZGC vs CMS) and explain how you detect and fix memory leaks in production.

JVM Garbage Collectors employ different algorithms to reclaim unreachable object memory:

  • CMS (Concurrent Mark Sweep): Deprecated in Java 9, removed in 14. An older, multi-threaded collector that marks and sweeps concurrently with application execution. Suffered from heap fragmentation (no compaction phase), leading to unpredictable Stop-The-World (STW) pauses.
  • G1GC (Garbage First): The default collector since Java 9. The heap is split into equal-sized virtual regions. G1GC targets regions with the most garbage first (hence 'Garbage First') and compacts memory incrementally. It allows configuring target STW pause limits (e.g., `-XX:MaxGCPauseMillis=200`).
  • ZGC (Z Garbage Collector): Production-ready in Java 15. A scalable, ultra-low latency collector. It performs all heavy GC phases concurrently with application threads (marking, compaction, reference processing) using **load barriers** and **colored pointers**. STW pauses are guaranteed to be under 1ms, regardless of heap size (from megabytes to terabytes).

Detecting and Fixing Memory Leaks:

A memory leak in Java occurs when unused objects remain reachable in the GC root chain (e.g., static collections retaining records, unclosed resources, listeners).

  1. Detection Tools: Use APMs (Datadog, Dynatrace), JVM utilities (`jmap`, `jstat`, `jcmd`), or profilers (VisualVM, JProfiler, Eclipse MAT).
  2. Process: Generate a Heap Dump when memory usage is high (`jmap -dump:format=b,file=heap.hprof `). Open it in Eclipse MAT and run a "Leak Suspects" report to find which class loaders or objects dominate Heap memory.
  3. Fixes: Make sure static caches have size limits, remove listeners/callbacks, wrap resources in try-with-resources, and prefer weak/soft references (WeakHashMap) for caching.

Explain the JVM Memory Model. What are Heap, Stack, Metaspace, and the difference between PermGen and Metaspace?

The JVM splits runtime data memory into several distinct areas:

  • Stack Memory: Thread-local execution space. Stored in LIFO order, containing local variables, primitive values, and reference handles to objects on the Heap. Stack memory is allocated per thread; once a method finishes execution, its stack frame is instantly popped and cleared. Thread stacks do not undergo Garbage Collection.
  • Heap Memory: Global shared memory space where all Java objects, arrays, and instance variables are allocated. The Heap is divided into the Young Generation (Eden, Survivor spaces S0/S1) and Old/Tenured Generation. Young Gen holds short-lived allocations (Minor GC), while long-lived objects are promoted to Old Gen (Major/Full GC).
  • Metaspace (Java 8+): Replaced the old **PermGen** (Permanent Generation). It stores class definitions, method metadata, runtime constant pool, and annotations.

Difference: PermGen vs Metaspace
  • Memory Allocation: PermGen was part of the contiguous JVM Heap, with a fixed max size (`-XX:MaxPermSize`). Sizing errors frequently caused `java.lang.OutOfMemoryError: PermGen space`. Metaspace is allocated from **native off-heap memory** (unlimited by default, bounded only by OS physical memory). You can cap it using `-XX:MaxMetaspaceSize`.
  • Garbage Collection: Metaspace undergoes class unloading and garbage collection automatically once class loader instances become unreachable.
MemoryDiagnostics.java 
public class MemoryDiagnostics {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        Runtime runtime = Runtime.getRuntime();
        long maxMemory = runtime.maxMemory(); // -Xmx
        long allocatedMemory = runtime.totalMemory();
        long freeMemory = runtime.freeMemory();

        System.out.println("Max JVM Heap Limit (bytes): " + maxMemory);
        System.out.println("Allocated Heap Space (bytes): " + allocatedMemory);
        System.out.println("Free Heap Space in Allocated (bytes): " + freeMemory);
        System.out.println("Used Heap Space (bytes): " + (allocatedMemory - freeMemory));
    }
}

What is a deadlock? How do you prevent it?

Core Answer: In response to the query about a deadlock? how do you prevent it, Deadlock occurs when two threads wait on each other to release locks. Prevent using lock ordering, timeout mechanisms, or tryLock.

Production Standards: For multithreaded JVM modules dealing with deadlock, guarantee thread safety using thread-safe primitives, avoid sharing mutable state without proper locking, and prevent deadlock scenarios.

Observability & Monitoring: Analyze thread contention, CPU lock blocks, and JVM thread allocations related to deadlock using thread dumps or Java Flight Recorder (JFR).

Performance Guidelines: Reduce lock overhead bottlenecks for deadlock by narrowing synchronization blocks, using lock-free data structures, and sizing thread executor pools appropriately.

DeadlockDemo.java 
import java.util.concurrent.locks.ReentrantLock;
import java.util.concurrent.TimeUnit;

public class DeadlockDemo {
    private final ReentrantLock lockA = new ReentrantLock();
    private final ReentrantLock lockB = new ReentrantLock();

    public void executeTask() throws InterruptedException {
        // tryLock avoids thread deadlock by returning false if a lock cannot be acquired within 500ms
        if (lockA.tryLock(500, TimeUnit.MILLISECONDS)) {
            try {
                if (lockB.tryLock(500, TimeUnit.MILLISECONDS)) {
                    try {
                        System.out.println("Finished safely!");
                    } finally {
                        lockB.unlock();
                    }
                }
            } finally {
                lockA.unlock();
            }
        }
    }
}

ThreadContext.java 
public class ThreadContext {

    public static void setContext(String val) { context.set(val); }
    public static String getContext() { return context.get(); }

When should you use Callable over Runnable?

TaskExecution.java 
import java.util.concurrent.Callable;
import java.util.concurrent.ExecutorService;
import java.util.concurrent.Executors;
import java.util.concurrent.Future;

public class TaskExecution {
    public static void main(String[] args) throws Exception {
        ExecutorService executor = Executors.newSingleThreadExecutor();
        // Callable yields Future output and handles checked exceptions
        Callable<Integer> task = () -> { Thread.sleep(100); return 42; };
        Future<Integer> f = executor.submit(task);
        System.out.println("Result: " + f.get());
        executor.shutdown();
    }
}

When to use ConcurrentHashMap vs Collections.synchronizedMap()?

ConcurrentMap.java 
import java.util.concurrent.ConcurrentHashMap;

public class ConcurrentMap {

        // CAS and individual bucket monitor locks allow high write concurrency
        ConcurrentHashMap<String, Integer> map = new ConcurrentHashMap<>();

        map.computeIfPresent("active-sessions", (k, v) -> v + 1);

What is PermGen vs Metaspace?

Core Answer: In response to the query about permgen vs metaspace, PermGen (pre-Java 8) was fixed-size. Metaspace grows dynamically.

Production Standards: For multithreaded JVM modules dealing with permgen, guarantee thread safety using thread-safe primitives, avoid sharing mutable state without proper locking, and prevent deadlock scenarios.

Observability & Monitoring: Analyze thread contention, CPU lock blocks, and JVM thread allocations related to permgen using thread dumps or Java Flight Recorder (JFR).

Performance Guidelines: Reduce lock overhead bottlenecks for permgen by narrowing synchronization blocks, using lock-free data structures, and sizing thread executor pools appropriately.

MetaspaceTuning.sh 
#!/bin/bash
# Limit Metaspace sizing to avoid native memory leaks inside containers
java -XX:MetaspaceSize=128m -XX:MaxMetaspaceSize=512m -jar billing.jar

What’s a deadlock in DB?

Core Answer: In response to the query about what’s a deadlock in db, Two transactions waiting on each other’s locks.

Production Standards: For multithreaded JVM modules dealing with deadlock, guarantee thread safety using thread-safe primitives, avoid sharing mutable state without proper locking, and prevent deadlock scenarios.

Observability & Monitoring: Analyze thread contention, CPU lock blocks, and JVM thread allocations related to deadlock using thread dumps or Java Flight Recorder (JFR).

Performance Guidelines: Reduce lock overhead bottlenecks for deadlock by narrowing synchronization blocks, using lock-free data structures, and sizing thread executor pools appropriately.

DeadlockDemo.java 
import java.util.concurrent.locks.ReentrantLock;
import java.util.concurrent.TimeUnit;

public class DeadlockDemo {
    private final ReentrantLock lockA = new ReentrantLock();
    private final ReentrantLock lockB = new ReentrantLock();

    public void executeTask() throws InterruptedException {
        // tryLock avoids thread deadlock by returning false if a lock cannot be acquired within 500ms
        if (lockA.tryLock(500, TimeUnit.MILLISECONDS)) {
            try {
                if (lockB.tryLock(500, TimeUnit.MILLISECONDS)) {
                    try {
                        System.out.println("Finished safely!");
                    } finally {
                        lockB.unlock();
                    }
                }
            } finally {
                lockA.unlock();
            }
        }
    }
}

hash map va concurrenthashmap

HashMap vs ConcurrentHashMap: HashMap is non-thread-safe and performs unsynchronized operations on its internal bucket array (Node[]). Concurrent modifications can lead to race conditions, data corruption, or infinite loops (in pre-Java 8). ConcurrentHashMap guarantees thread-safety without lock contention by utilizing fine-grained locking: CAS (Compare-And-Swap) for empty buckets and bucket-level synchronization (locking only the head Node of a specific bucket) during writes, while reads are completely lock-free.

Production Standards: For shared state in multi-threaded contexts, always use ConcurrentHashMap instead of wrapping a standard map via Collections.synchronizedMap(), as the latter locks the entire collection, severely limiting scalability.

Observability & Monitoring: Monitor thread contention and lock wait times using Java Flight Recorder (JFR) under high concurrency to identify bucket bottlenecks or excessive hash collisions.

Performance Guidelines: Set an appropriate initial capacity to minimize rehash operations. Use atomic compound operations like computeIfAbsent, merge, or putIfAbsent to prevent race conditions during read-modify-write workflows.

ConcurrentMapPerformance.java 
import java.util.concurrent.ConcurrentHashMap;
import java.util.concurrent.ExecutorService;
import java.util.concurrent.Executors;
import java.util.concurrent.TimeUnit;
import java.util.concurrent.atomic.LongAdder;

public class ConcurrentMapPerformance {
    // Thread-safe map to store access counts per endpoint URL
    private static final ConcurrentHashMap<String, LongAdder> statsMap = new ConcurrentHashMap<>();

    public static void logRequest(String url) {
        // Atomic computeIfAbsent: creates LongAdder if URL key is missing
        statsMap.computeIfAbsent(url, k -> new LongAdder()).increment();
    }

    public static void main(String[] args) throws InterruptedException {
        ExecutorService executor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(10);

        // Simulate 1000 concurrent requests hitting three endpoints
        for (int i = 0; i < 1000; i++) {
            final int index = i;
            executor.submit(() -> {
                String url = "/api/v1/resource/" + (index % 3);
                logRequest(url);
            });
        }

        executor.shutdown();
        executor.awaitTermination(5, TimeUnit.SECONDS);

        // Display results
        statsMap.forEach((url, adder) -> 
            System.out.println(url + " -> Requests: " + adder.sum())
        );
    }
}

static vs final vs volatile vs transient

Core Keywords Lifecycle: In the JVM runtime, static indicates a member belongs to the class itself rather than instances, loaded once into JVM Metaspace/Classloader memory. final restricts modifications, making fields immutable, methods non-overrideable, and classes non-inheritable. volatile guarantees thread-memory visibility, forcing all reads/writes directly to main memory and bypassing CPU registers to prevent stale local cache reads. transient excludes fields from Java's built-in Serialization framework.

Production Standards: Use static final for global constants. Enforce design-level class and object immutability using final. Limit volatile strictly to simple state flags, and mark sensitive data (e.g. passwords, API keys) as transient to prevent serialization leakage.

Observability & Monitoring: Monitor Metaspace consumption and heap growth using garbage collection logs to track static object leaks. Track thread contention and synchronization bottlenecks associated with memory barriers.

Performance Guidelines: Avoid storing large mutable datasets in static fields, as they escape standard garbage collection lifetimes. Minimize read frequencies on volatile fields in heavy loops by caching values in local stack variables.

KeywordsLifecycleDemo.java 
import java.io.Serializable;

public class KeywordsLifecycleDemo implements Serializable {
    private static final long serialVersionUID = 1L;

    // 1. static final: Class-level immutable constant
    public static final String APP_ID = "CORE-PAYMENT-SERVICE";

    // 2. final: Instance-level constant, must be initialized
    private final long initializedTime;

    // 3. volatile: Memory visibility guaranteed for cross-thread flag
    private volatile boolean isRunning = true;

    // 4. transient: Excluded from serialization output
    private transient String decryptedSessionToken;

    public KeywordsLifecycleDemo(String token) {
        this.initializedTime = System.currentTimeMillis();
        this.decryptedSessionToken = token;
    }

    public void stopService() {
        this.isRunning = false; // Write immediately visible to other threads
    }

    public boolean checkStatus() {
        return this.isRunning; // Read directly from main memory
    }

    public String getToken() {
        return decryptedSessionToken;
    }
}
Architecture & Clean Code

OOP & Design Patterns

Four pillars of OOP, SOLID principles (particularly LSP, DIP, ISP), and GoF design patterns (Singleton, Factory, Strategy, Builder, Observer, Proxy, Decorator, Adapter, Template Method, State, Workflow Engine).

Explain the Factory and Singleton design patterns. Write a thread-safe Singleton class in Java.

Factory Pattern: Encapsulates object creation logic, returning objects based on input parameters.

Singleton Pattern: Ensures a class has only one instance per JVM classloader and provides a global access point.

Thread Safety: Thread-safe Singleton implementation requires double-checked locking with a volatile instance variable or using the Bill Pugh Helper class.

Bill Pugh Method: Uses a private static inner helper class that is loaded only when getInstance() is called, ensuring lazy evaluation and thread-safety.

SingletonAndFactoryDemo.java 
public class SingletonAndFactoryDemo {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        // Singleton Usage
        ThreadSafeSingleton s1 = ThreadSafeSingleton.getInstance();
        ThreadSafeSingleton s2 = ThreadSafeSingleton.getInstance();
        System.out.println("Same Singleton Instance: " + (s1 == s2));

        // Factory Usage
        Shape s = ShapeFactory.getShape("CIRCLE");
        s.draw();
    }
}

// 1. Thread-safe Singleton using Bill Pugh Inner Helper Class
class ThreadSafeSingleton {
    private ThreadSafeSingleton() {} // Prevent direct instantiation

    private static class SingletonHelper {
        private static final ThreadSafeSingleton INSTANCE = new ThreadSafeSingleton();
    }

    public static ThreadSafeSingleton getInstance() {
        return SingletonHelper.INSTANCE; // Lazy loaded and thread-safe
    }
}

// 2. Factory Pattern Example
interface Shape { void draw(); }
class Circle implements Shape { public void draw() { System.out.println("Circle drawn."); } }
class Square implements Shape { public void draw() { System.out.println("Square drawn."); } }

class ShapeFactory {
    public static Shape getShape(String type) {
        if ("CIRCLE".equalsIgnoreCase(type)) return new Circle();
        if ("SQUARE".equalsIgnoreCase(type)) return new Square();
        throw new IllegalArgumentException("Unknown shape: " + type);
    }
}

Explain Loose Coupling and the SOLID design principles in software architecture.

Loose Coupling: Minimizes direct dependencies between classes by using interfaces and Dependency Injection.

Single Responsibility (S) & Open/Closed (O): S: A class should have one reason to change. O: Open for extension, closed for modification.

Liskov Substitution (L) & Interface Segregation (I): L: Subclasses should be replaceable for parent classes. I: Prefer small, client-specific interfaces.

Dependency Inversion (D): Depend on abstractions (interfaces) rather than concrete implementations, supporting application maintenance.

SolidDesignDemo.java 
// 1. Dependency Inversion Principle (DIP) - Interface Abstraction
interface Engine { void start(); }

class CombustionEngine implements Engine {
    @Override
    public void start() { System.out.println("Vroom... Combustion engine started."); }
}

class ElectricEngine implements Engine {
    @Override
    public void start() { System.out.println("Silent hum... Electric engine started."); }
}

// Car is loosely coupled - depends on Engine interface, not concrete engines
class Car {
    private final Engine engine;

    // Dependency injected via constructor
    public Car(Engine engine) {
        this.engine = engine;
    }

    public void drive() {
        engine.start();
        System.out.println("Car is moving...");
    }
}

What is Idempotence in API design? How do you implement idempotent POST endpoints?

Idempotence: Ensures that making multiple identical requests has the same effect as making a single request.

HTTP Methods: Safe methods (GET, HEAD) and write methods (PUT, DELETE) are idempotent. `POST` is non-idempotent by default.

Idempotent POST: Require clients to submit a unique transaction key (Idempotency-Key header) with the request.

Redis Check: The API checks this key in Redis. If found, it returns the cached response; if not, it processes the request and caches the result.

IdempotencyFilter.java 
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;
import jakarta.servlet.*;
import jakarta.servlet.http.HttpServletRequest;
import jakarta.servlet.http.HttpServletResponse;
import java.io.IOException;

@Component
public class IdempotencyFilter implements Filter {
    
    // Mock Cache - Use redisTemplate.opsForValue() in production
    private final java.util.Map<String, String> idempotencyCache = new java.util.concurrent.ConcurrentHashMap<>();

    @Override
    public void doFilter(ServletRequest request, ServletResponse response, FilterChain chain)
            throws IOException, ServletException {
        HttpServletRequest httpRequest = (HttpServletRequest) request;
        HttpServletResponse httpResponse = (HttpServletResponse) response;

        if ("POST".equalsIgnoreCase(httpRequest.getMethod())) {
            String key = httpRequest.getHeader("Idempotency-Key");
            
            if (key != null && !key.isEmpty()) {
                if (idempotencyCache.containsKey(key)) {
                    // Return cached response immediately, bypassing application filters
                    httpResponse.setStatus(HttpServletResponse.SC_CONFLICT);
                    httpResponse.getWriter().write("Duplicate request blocked. Prev Response: " + idempotencyCache.get(key));
                    return;
                }
                
                // Track request key (In production set TTL like 1 hour)
                idempotencyCache.put(key, "PROCESSED_SUCCESSFULLY");
            }
        }
        
        chain.doFilter(request, response);
    }
}

Design a pluggable, concurrent workflow execution engine. Combine Strategy, Factory, and State patterns to adhere to SOLID principles.

To design a highly extensible, concurrent workflow engine, we map these patterns to distinct responsibilities:

  1. Strategy Pattern: Encapsulates individual step execution algorithms. Adding a new task simply requires implementing a `TaskStrategy` interface.
  2. Factory Pattern: Instantiates the correct task execution strategy dynamically based on metadata definitions.
  3. State Pattern: Manages the lifecycle transitions of the Workflow instance (`PENDING`, `RUNNING`, `COMPLETED`, `FAILED`).
By combining these patterns, we prevent code bloat, isolate concerns, and make the workflow engine fully compliant with the **Open-Closed Principle** (adding new task types without editing core execution engines).

WorkflowEngine.java 
import java.util.HashMap;
import java.util.Map;
import java.util.concurrent.ExecutorService;
import java.util.concurrent.Executors;

// State Interface
interface WorkflowState {
    void handle(WorkflowContext ctx);
}

class RunningState implements WorkflowState {
    @Override
    public void handle(WorkflowContext ctx) {
        System.out.println("Workflow is running tasks concurrently...");
    }
}

class CompletedState implements WorkflowState {
    @Override
    public void handle(WorkflowContext ctx) {
        System.out.println("Workflow finalized successfully. Cleaning up resources.");
    }
}

class WorkflowContext {
    private WorkflowState state = new RunningState();
    public void setState(WorkflowState state) { this.state = state; }
    public void execute() { state.handle(this); }
}

// Strategy Interface for Tasks
interface TaskStrategy {
    void executeTask(String payload);
}

class EmailTask implements TaskStrategy {
    @Override public void executeTask(String p) { System.out.println("Sending email: " + p); }
}

class DbWriteTask implements TaskStrategy {
    @Override public void executeTask(String p) { System.out.println("Persisting: " + p); }
}

// Factory to resolve Strategy
class TaskFactory {
    private static final Map<String, TaskStrategy> strategies = new HashMap<>();
    static {
        strategies.put("EMAIL", new EmailTask());
        strategies.put("DB", new DbWriteTask());
    }
    public static TaskStrategy get(String type) {
        return strategies.getOrDefault(type, p -> System.out.println("No-Op Task"));
    }
}

// Orchestrator Engine
public class WorkflowEngine {
    private final ExecutorService executor = Executors.newFixedThreadPool(4);

    public void processWorkflow(String taskType, String payload) {
        WorkflowContext ctx = new WorkflowContext();
        ctx.execute(); // Enter Running State

        executor.submit(() -> {
            TaskStrategy strategy = TaskFactory.get(taskType);
            strategy.executeTask(payload);
            
            ctx.setState(new CompletedState());
            ctx.execute(); // Transition to Completed State
        });
    }
    
    public void shutdown() { executor.shutdown(); }
}

Outline Singleton, Factory vs Abstract Factory, Strategy, Builder, Observer, Proxy, Decorator, and Adapter patterns.

These Gang of Four (GoF) patterns form the core of reusable object-oriented architectures:

  • Singleton: Restricts class instantiation to a single instance. Must prevent double-instantiation via multi-threading (using double-checked locking or JVM ClassLoader-based holder/enums).
  • Factory Method vs Abstract Factory: Factory Method uses inheritance to let subclasses decide which product to instantiate. Abstract Factory uses composition to create families of related products without specifying concrete classes.
  • Strategy: Defines a family of interchangeable algorithms and injects them dynamically. Promotes composition over inheritance.
  • Builder: Separates complex object construction from its representation, allowing step-by-step object assembly (essential for immutable structures).
  • Observer: Sets up a one-to-many subscription model so dependent objects receive automatic notifications when state changes.
  • Proxy vs Decorator vs Adapter:
    • **Proxy**: Controls access to the target object (e.g., lazy loading, security, caching, transactional aspects). Has the exact same interface.
    • **Decorator**: Adds responsibilities to an object dynamically without changing its interface.
    • **Adapter**: Converts an incompatible interface into another interface expected by client code.

Give a concrete example of a violation of the Liskov Substitution Principle (LSP) and how you refactored it using SOLID principles.

The Liskov Substitution Principle (LSP) states that subtype objects must be substitutable for base type objects without altering correctness or breaking client expectations. A classic violation occurs when a subclass overrides a base class method but throws `UnsupportedOperationException`, narrows preconditions, or relaxes postconditions.

For example, if class `Bird` defines `fly()` and we write `Ostrich extends Bird`, overriding `fly()` to throw an exception, client code iterating over a list of `Bird` objects will break. We refactor this using:

  • Interface Segregation Principle (ISP): Split the interface into separate capabilities (`Flyable` and `NonFlyable` or just extract flying).
  • Dependency Inversion Principle (DIP): Program to abstractions, letting classes inject the capabilities they actually support.

SOLIDRefactoring.java 
// 🛑 LSP Violation Example:
// class Ostrich extends Bird { @Override public void fly() { throw new UnsupportedOperationException(); } }

// ✅ Refactored Solution using ISP and Composition
interface Flyable {
    void fly();
}

interface Walkable {
    void walk();
}

class Sparrow implements Flyable, Walkable {
    @Override public void fly() { System.out.println("Sparrow is flying."); }
    @Override public void walk() { System.out.println("Sparrow is walking."); }
}

class Ostrich implements Walkable {
    @Override public void walk() { System.out.println("Ostrich is walking fast."); }
}

// Client application interacts only with interfaces they require
public class SOLIDRefactoring {
    public static void makeFly(Flyable f) {
        f.fly(); // Safe, cannot receive an Ostrich here!
    }
}

What are the 4 pillars of OOP in Java?

Core Answer: In response to the query about 4 pillars of oop in java, Encapsulation, Abstraction, Inheritance, Polymorphism.

Production Standards: For Spring Boot modules utilizing OopPrinciples, enforce proper layering (Controller, Service, Repository), leverage dependency injection (IoC), and separate profiles configuration.

Observability & Monitoring: Track bean creation steps, request handler latencies, and transaction boundary status for OopPrinciples using Spring Boot Actuator and SLF4J logs.

Performance Guidelines: Streamline resource consumption for OopPrinciples by utilizing lazy bean loading, minimizing database connection times, and configuring connection pool limits.

OopPrinciples.java 
abstract class Animal {
    private String name; // Encapsulation
    public Animal(String n) { this.name = n; }
    
    public abstract void speak(); // Abstraction
}

class Dog extends Animal { // Inheritance
    public Dog(String n) { super(n); }
    @Override public void speak() { System.out.println("Bark"); } // Polymorphism
}

What is the Liskov Substitution Principle?

LspCompliance.java 
// Subclasses must remain substituteable for their base classes
public class Rectangle {
    protected int width; protected int height;
    public void setWidth(int w) { this.width = w; }
    public void setHeight(int h) { this.height = h; }
}

What is the Singleton pattern? How do you implement it in Java?

Core Answer: In response to the query about singleton pattern? how do you implement it in java, Ensures only one instance exists. Implement via private constructor and static method.

Production Standards: For Spring Boot modules utilizing SingletonDemo, enforce proper layering (Controller, Service, Repository), leverage dependency injection (IoC), and separate profiles configuration.

Observability & Monitoring: Track bean creation steps, request handler latencies, and transaction boundary status for SingletonDemo using Spring Boot Actuator and SLF4J logs.

Performance Guidelines: Streamline resource consumption for SingletonDemo by utilizing lazy bean loading, minimizing database connection times, and configuring connection pool limits.

SingletonDemo.java 
public class SingletonDemo {
    private static volatile SingletonDemo instance;
    private SingletonDemo() {}

    // Double-checked locking thread-safe implementation
    public static SingletonDemo getInstance() {
        if (instance == null) {
            synchronized (SingletonDemo.class) {
                if (instance == null) {
                    instance = new SingletonDemo();
                }
            }
        }
        return instance;
    }
}

BuilderPattern.java 
public class BuilderPattern {
    private final String host; private final int port;

    private BuilderPattern(Builder b) { this.host = b.host; this.port = b.port; }

    public static class Builder {
        private String host; private int port;
        public Builder host(String h) { this.host = h; return this; }
        public Builder port(int p) { this.port = p; return this; }
        public BuilderPattern build() { return new BuilderPattern(this); }

What is the Observer pattern?

Core Answer: In response to the query about observer pattern, Allows subscribers to react to changes in state (e.g., event listeners).

ObserverPattern.java 
import java.util.ArrayList;
import java.util.List;

interface Observer { void update(String msg); }

public class ObserverPattern {
    private final List<Observer> observers = new ArrayList<>();
    
    public void register(Observer o) { observers.add(o); }
    public void notifyAll(String msg) {
        observers.forEach(o -> o.update(msg));
    }
}

When do you use Command pattern?

Core Answer: In response to the query about when do you use command pattern, For encapsulating a request as an object (undo/redo systems).

CommandPatternService.java 
package com.valore.enterprise;

import org.slf4j.Logger;
import org.slf4j.LoggerFactory;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;

@Component
public class CommandPatternService {
    private static final Logger log = LoggerFactory.getLogger(CommandPatternService.class);

    public void processTask() {

What pattern does Java’s Enum Singleton use?

Core Answer: In response to the query about what pattern does java’s enum singleton use, Enum is the safest way to implement Singleton, guards against reflection and serialization.

Production Standards: For Spring Boot modules utilizing SingletonDemo, enforce proper layering (Controller, Service, Repository), leverage dependency injection (IoC), and separate profiles configuration.

Observability & Monitoring: Track bean creation steps, request handler latencies, and transaction boundary status for SingletonDemo using Spring Boot Actuator and SLF4J logs.

SingletonDemo.java 
public class SingletonDemo {
    private static volatile SingletonDemo instance;
    private SingletonDemo() {}

    // Double-checked locking thread-safe implementation
    public static SingletonDemo getInstance() {
        if (instance == null) {
            synchronized (SingletonDemo.class) {
                if (instance == null) {
                    instance = new SingletonDemo();
                }
            }
        }
        return instance;
    }
}
