Inversion of Control

Spring Core & AOP

ApplicationContext vs BeanFactory, bean lifecycle and scopes (gotchas with prototype in singleton), Dependency Injection patterns, Aspect-Oriented Programming (Aspect, Advice, Weaving), and proxy self-invocation gotchas.

Why is Spring AOP used in enterprise applications? Show how to create a custom aspect to log method executions.

Aspect-Oriented Programming: Spring AOP decouples cross-cutting concerns (logging, transaction management, security checks) from core business logic, preventing duplicate code.

Proxy Architecture: AOP wraps target beans in dynamic proxies at runtime (using JDK dynamic proxies or CGLIB). Calls are intercepted to run advice before delegating to the target method.

Self-Invocation gotcha: When a bean invokes its own method internally, the call bypasses the proxy container, meaning aspects (like @Transactional or custom logging aspects) will not execute.

Aspect Mechanics: Aspects are defined using pointcut expressions that identify execution targets, and advice (like @Before or @Around) that determines when the aspect logic runs.

ExecutionTimeAspect.java 
import org.aspectj.lang.ProceedingJoinPoint;
import org.aspectj.lang.annotation.Around;
import org.aspectj.lang.annotation.Aspect;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;

@Aspect
@Component
public class ExecutionTimeAspect {

    // Aspect intercepts all methods inside services package
    @Around("execution(* com.valore.grocery.service..*(..))")
    public Object profileMethod(ProceedingJoinPoint joinPoint) throws Throwable {
        long start = System.currentTimeMillis();
        
        try {
            // Execute actual service method
            return joinPoint.proceed();
        } finally {
            long executionTime = System.currentTimeMillis() - start;
            System.out.println(joinPoint.getSignature() + " executed in " + executionTime + "ms");
        }
    }
}

Explain the roles of @Qualifier and @Override annotations. Compare @NoArgsConstructor and @AllArgsConstructor in Lombok.

@Qualifier: Resolves injection ambiguity when multiple beans of the same type exist in the context. By pairing it with @Autowired, you specify the exact bean name to inject.

@Override: A compiler-checked annotation ensuring a subclass method signature matches a method in the superclass or interface, preventing accidental signature modifications.

@NoArgsConstructor: Generates a zero-argument constructor, which is necessary for JPA entities (Hibernate proxy generation) and JSON serialization.

@AllArgsConstructor: Generates a constructor containing parameters for all fields, enabling clean constructor-based Dependency Injection in Spring components.

ConstructorInjectionQualifierDemo.java 
import org.springframework.beans.factory.annotation.Qualifier;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;
import lombok.AllArgsConstructor;
import lombok.NoArgsConstructor;

interface NotificationSender { void send(String msg); }

@Component("emailSender")
class EmailSender implements NotificationSender {
    @Override // Forces compilation validation for overrides
    public void send(String msg) { System.out.println("Email: " + msg); }
}

@Component("smsSender")
class SmsSender implements NotificationSender {
    @Override
    public void send(String msg) { System.out.println("SMS: " + msg); }
}

@Component
@AllArgsConstructor // Generates constructor for injection (no @Autowired needed on constructor)
class AlertService {
    
    // Disambiguate using @Qualifier
    @Qualifier("emailSender")
    private final NotificationSender emailNotifier;
    
    @Qualifier("smsSender")
    private final NotificationSender smsNotifier;

    public void triggerAlert(String text) {
        emailNotifier.send(text);
        smsNotifier.send(text);
    }
}

What is the difference between Spring's Singleton and Prototype scopes? How do you resolve the prototype-in-singleton injection gotcha?

Singleton Scope: Spring's default scope. The container creates exactly one bean instance per IoC context. Subsequent requests return the cached instance, which requires stateless implementations to ensure thread safety.

Prototype Scope: Instructs the container to create a new instance of the bean every time it is requested, making it suitable for stateful business workflows.

Scoped Bean Gotcha: When a prototype bean is injected into a singleton bean, it is only injected once during the singleton's initialization. The singleton bean continues to reuse that initial instance, preventing updates.

Resolution Strategies: Resolve this by accessing the prototype bean programmatically using ObjectProvider<T>, or by annotating a getter method with @Lookup to fetch a new instance from the container at runtime.

PrototypeInjectionSolution.java 
import org.springframework.beans.factory.ObjectProvider;
import org.springframework.beans.factory.annotation.Autowired;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Scope;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;

@Component
@Scope("prototype")
class TaskProcessor {
    private double taskId = Math.random();
    public void process() {
        System.out.println("Processing Task ID: " + taskId);
    }
}

@Component
class SingletonManager {
    // Injected provider - allows fetching fresh prototype instances on demand
    @Autowired
    private ObjectProvider<TaskProcessor> processorProvider;

    public void runTask() {
        // Fetch a brand new TaskProcessor instance every time this method runs
        TaskProcessor processor = processorProvider.getObject();
        processor.process();
    }
}

Explain Inversion of Control (IoC) vs Dependency Injection (DI). Compare BeanFactory vs ApplicationContext.

Inversion of Control (IoC): IoC is an architectural design principle where control over object creation, configuration, and lifecycle management is transferred from the application code to an external container or framework.

Dependency Injection (DI): DI is the implementation pattern used to realize IoC. Instead of objects instantiating their dependencies directly, dependencies are injected into them by the Spring container via constructor, setter, or field injection.

BeanFactory: The foundational IoC interface in Spring. It lazy-loads beans by default, features a small memory footprint, and provides basic configuration support. It is suitable for resource-constrained environments.

ApplicationContext: Extends BeanFactory, loading all singleton beans eagerly at startup. It adds advanced enterprise features like internationalization (i18n), event publishing, WebApplicationContext bindings, and seamless integration with AOP.

IocDiDemoConfig.java 
import org.springframework.context.ApplicationContext;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.AnnotationConfigApplicationContext;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Bean;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Configuration;

@Configuration
class AppConfig {
    @Bean
    public PaymentService paymentService() {
        return new CreditCardPaymentService(); // Dependency Instantiation delegated to Spring
    }
}

interface PaymentService { void pay(); }
class CreditCardPaymentService implements PaymentService {
    @Override public void pay() { System.out.println("Processing credit card charge..."); }
}

public class IocDiDemoConfig {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        // Instantiate Spring ApplicationContext container
        ApplicationContext context = new AnnotationConfigApplicationContext(AppConfig.class);
        
        // Retrieve bean from IoC container (DI resolves dependency)
        PaymentService service = context.getBean(PaymentService.class);
        service.pay();
    }
}

Explain the self-invocation proxy gotcha in Spring AOP and how to bypass it. Show a custom Aspect for distributed rate limiting.

Spring AOP is proxy-based. When a client bean calls a method on another target bean, Spring routes the call through an intermediate proxy, executing any woven aspects (like `@Transactional` or custom rate limiters). However, if a bean calls one of its own internal methods (self-invocation), the call bypasses the proxy entirely, resolving to direct JVM `this` reference calls. As a result, **no aspect logic executes**.

Bypassing Self-Invocation:
  1. Self-Injection (Recommended): Inject the bean into itself using lazy autowiring. Internal calls are made on the self-injected variable, routing through the proxy.
  2. AopContext.currentProxy(): Access the proxy directly via `((MyService) AopContext.currentProxy()).internalMethod()`. Requires setting `@EnableAspectJAutoProxy(exposeProxy = true)`.
  3. Refactoring: Move the self-invoked method to a separate helper helper service bean.
RateLimiterAspect.java 
import org.aspectj.lang.ProceedingJoinPoint;
import org.aspectj.lang.annotation.Around;
import org.aspectj.lang.annotation.Aspect;
import org.aspectj.lang.annotation.Pointcut;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Lazy;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;
import java.util.concurrent.ConcurrentHashMap;

@Aspect
@Component
public class RateLimiterAspect {
    private final ConcurrentHashMap<String, Long> clientRequestTimestamps = new ConcurrentHashMap<>();
    private final MyService self;

    // Self-injection to bypass self-invocation gotcha if needed!
    public RateLimiterAspect(@Lazy MyService self) {
        this.self = self;
    }

    @Pointcut("@annotation(RateLimit)")
    public void rateLimitedMethod() {}

    @Around("rateLimitedMethod()")
    public Object enforceLimit(ProceedingJoinPoint joinPoint) throws Throwable {
        // Mocking client tracking (In production, retrieve IP/JWT token from SecurityContext)
        String clientIp = "127.0.0.1"; 
        
        long currentTime = System.currentTimeMillis();
        long lastRequest = clientRequestTimestamps.getOrDefault(clientIp, 0L);

        if (currentTime - lastRequest < 1000) { // Limit: 1 request per second
            throw new RuntimeException("HTTP 429: Too Many Requests. Rate limit exceeded.");
        }

        clientRequestTimestamps.put(clientIp, currentTime);
        return joinPoint.proceed(); // Safe execution
    }
}

What is Inversion of Control (IoC) and Dependency Injection (DI)? Compare BeanFactory vs ApplicationContext and Bean Scopes.

IoC (Inversion of Control) is the design principal where control over object creation, configuration, and lifecycles is inverted and handed over to a container framework. Dependency Injection (DI) is the concrete implementation pattern used to supply dependencies to objects. Spring supports Field, Setter, and Constructor injection. **Constructor injection is the industry best practice** because it ensures dependencies are immutable, facilitates unit testing without mocking containers, and prevents circular dependencies at startup.

BeanFactory vs ApplicationContext
  • BeanFactory: The root Spring container interface. Employs **Lazy Loading** (instantiates beans only when `getBean()` is explicitly requested). Extremely lightweight, suitable for memory-constrained environments.
  • ApplicationContext: Inherits from BeanFactory. Employs **Eager Loading** (pre-instantiates all singleton beans during startup). Extends functionality with advanced enterprise integrations: event propagation, internalization (i18n), AOP integration, and environment profile configurations.
Bean Scopes:
  • singleton: (Default) Exactly one instance of the bean is created per Spring IoC container.
  • prototype: A new bean instance is created every single time it is requested from the container.
  • request / session / application: Web-aware scopes bound to the lifecycle of HTTP requests, HTTP sessions, and ServletContext respectively.
Gotcha: Prototype Inside Singleton

If a prototype-scoped bean is injected directly (e.g. via @Autowired) into a singleton-scoped bean, **it behaves like a singleton**. The singleton bean is initialized only once, meaning the prototype bean is also injected only once. To resolve this, use @Lookup method injection or inject a Java Provider<T> / ObjectFactory<T> to fetch fresh instances dynamically.

PrototypeLookupPattern.java 
import org.springframework.beans.factory.annotation.Lookup;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Scope;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;

@Component
@Scope("prototype")
class RequestTracker {
    public void logRequest(String msg) {
        System.out.println("Tracker: " + this.hashCode() + " | " + msg);
    }
}

@Component
public class RequestService {
    // Spring dynamically overrides this method to retrieve a new prototype bean instance
    @Lookup
    public RequestTracker getTracker() {
        return null; // Implemented by Spring proxy
    }

    public void process(String requestData) {
        RequestTracker tracker = getTracker(); // Fetches a fresh instance every time!
        tracker.logRequest(requestData);
    }
}

LifecycleBean.java 
import jakarta.annotation.PostConstruct;
import jakarta.annotation.PreDestroy;
import org.springframework.beans.factory.InitializingBean;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;

@Component
public class LifecycleBean implements InitializingBean {
    @PostConstruct
    public void init() { System.out.println("1. PostConstruct hook execution"); }

    @Override
    public void afterPropertiesSet() { System.out.println("2. afterPropertiesSet injection callback"); }

    @PreDestroy
    public void destroy() { System.out.println("3. PreDestroy cleanup hook"); }
}

How is Factory pattern different from Abstract Factory?

FactoryPatternDifferentAbstractFactoryService.java 
package com.valore.enterprise;

What is AOP? Common use cases?

Core Answer: In response to the query about aop? common use cases, Aspect-Oriented Programming for cross-cutting concerns like logging, transactions.

Production Standards: For Spring Boot modules utilizing LoggingAspect, enforce proper layering (Controller, Service, Repository), leverage dependency injection (IoC), and separate profiles configuration.

Observability & Monitoring: Track bean creation steps, request handler latencies, and transaction boundary status for LoggingAspect using Spring Boot Actuator and SLF4J logs.

Performance Guidelines: Streamline resource consumption for LoggingAspect by utilizing lazy bean loading, minimizing database connection times, and configuring connection pool limits.

LoggingAspect.java 
import org.aspectj.lang.annotation.Aspect;
import org.aspectj.lang.annotation.Around;
import org.aspectj.lang.ProceedingJoinPoint;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;

@Aspect
@Component
public class LoggingAspect {
    @Around("execution(* com.valore.service..*(..))")
    public Object profileMethod(ProceedingJoinPoint joinPoint) throws Throwable {
        long start = System.currentTimeMillis();
        try {
            return joinPoint.proceed();
        } finally {
            System.out.println(joinPoint.getSignature() + " duration: " + (System.currentTimeMillis() - start) + "ms");
        }
    }
}

@Configuration
public class ScopesConfig {

    @RequestScope // Bean lifecycle tied strictly to current HTTP Request

What is the purpose of the @Qualifier annotation in Spring Boot?

Core Answer: In response to the query about purpose of the @qualifier annotation in spring boot, Implement enterprise-grade Spring configurations, dependency injections, or aspects related to qualifier to support modular architecture and cross-cutting concerns.

Production Standards: For Spring Boot modules utilizing QualifierDI, enforce proper layering (Controller, Service, Repository), leverage dependency injection (IoC), and separate profiles configuration.

Observability & Monitoring: Track bean creation steps, request handler latencies, and transaction boundary status for QualifierDI using Spring Boot Actuator and SLF4J logs.

Performance Guidelines: Streamline resource consumption for QualifierDI by utilizing lazy bean loading, minimizing database connection times, and configuring connection pool limits.

QualifierDI.java 
import org.springframework.beans.factory.annotation.Qualifier;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;

interface Card {}
@Component("visa") class VisaCard implements Card {}
@Component("mastercard") class MasterCard implements Card {}

@Component
public class QualifierDI {
    private final Card card;
    public QualifierDI(@Qualifier("visa") Card c) { this.card = c; }
}

What Is Aspect-Oriented Programming?

AOP (Aspect-Oriented Programming) complements OOP by allowing developers to modularize cross-cutting concerns (e.g., logging, transaction management, caching, security) that slice across multiple business classes.

Key AOP Terminologies:
  • Aspect: The module containing the cross-cutting concern (e.g., a `TransactionAspect` class). Contains Pointcuts and Advices.
  • JoinPoint: A point during execution of the program (such as method execution or exception handling) where an aspect can be plugged in. In Spring AOP, a JoinPoint is **always method execution**.
  • Pointcut: A predicate expression matching JoinPoints. It defines *where* the advice should run (e.g., execution of any method in `com.service.*`).
  • Advice: Action taken by an aspect at a particular JoinPoint. Types: `@Before`, `@After`, `@AfterReturning`, `@AfterThrowing`, and `@Around` (most powerful: can block execution, modify return values, and handle exceptions).
Types of Weaving (Plugging aspects into application types):
  1. Compile-Time Weaving: Aspects are woven into Java bytecode at compilation using the AspectJ compiler (`ajc`). Has zero runtime performance overhead.
  2. Post-Compile Weaving (Binary Weaving): Woven into existing compiled `.class` or `.jar` files.
  3. Load-Time Weaving (LTW): Woven dynamically when classes are loaded into JVM memory by using a JVM Java Agent (`-javaagent:aspectjweaver.jar`).
  4. Runtime Weaving (Spring AOP Default): Uses dynamic proxies (JDK dynamic proxies for interfaces, CGLIB subclasses for concrete classes) to intercept method calls at execution time. Limited to public method calls initiated outside the bean.

What Are Aspect, Advice, Pointcut, and JoinPoint in AOP?

Core Answer: In response to the query about aspect, advice, pointcut, and joinpoint in aop, Implement enterprise-grade Spring configurations, dependency injections, or aspects related to aop to support modular architecture and cross-cutting concerns.

Production Standards: For Spring Boot modules utilizing LoggingAspect, enforce proper layering (Controller, Service, Repository), leverage dependency injection (IoC), and separate profiles configuration.

Observability & Monitoring: Track bean creation steps, request handler latencies, and transaction boundary status for LoggingAspect using Spring Boot Actuator and SLF4J logs.

Performance Guidelines: Streamline resource consumption for LoggingAspect by utilizing lazy bean loading, minimizing database connection times, and configuring connection pool limits.

LoggingAspect.java 
import org.aspectj.lang.annotation.Aspect;
import org.aspectj.lang.annotation.Around;
import org.aspectj.lang.ProceedingJoinPoint;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;

@Aspect
@Component
public class LoggingAspect {
    @Around("execution(* com.valore.service..*(..))")
    public Object profileMethod(ProceedingJoinPoint joinPoint) throws Throwable {
        long start = System.currentTimeMillis();
        try {
            return joinPoint.proceed();
        } finally {
            System.out.println(joinPoint.getSignature() + " duration: " + (System.currentTimeMillis() - start) + "ms");
        }
    }
}

What is your systematic process to handle bugs in a production microservice environment? Show how to correlate logs using MDC.

Systematic Process: (1) Catch alerts (e.g. Dynatrace, Sentry), (2) Correlate logs via Trace ID using ELK/Splunk, (3) Extract inputs to write a failing integration test, (4) Apply fix, verify via CI/CD, and monitor post-deployment metrics.

MDC (Mapped Diagnostic Context): MDC allows placing context variables (like traceId, userId) in a thread-local map, automatically printing them in every log statement.

Context Propagation: When calling external APIs, propagate this trace context in HTTP headers (e.g. X-B3-TraceId or W3C Trace Context) to maintain end-to-end trace visibility.

Resolution Time: This prevents developers from manually parsing disparate logs, shortening production outage resolution times.

LoggingFilter.java 
import jakarta.servlet.*;
import jakarta.servlet.http.HttpServletRequest;
import org.slf4j.MDC;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;
import java.io.IOException;
import java.util.UUID;

@Component
public class LoggingFilter implements Filter {
    private static final String TRACE_ID_KEY = "traceId";

    @Override
    public void doFilter(ServletRequest request, ServletResponse response, FilterChain chain)
            throws IOException, ServletException {
        HttpServletRequest httpRequest = (HttpServletRequest) request;
        
        // Extract traceId from incoming headers, or generate one if absent
        String traceId = httpRequest.getHeader("X-Correlation-ID");
        if (traceId == null || traceId.isEmpty()) {
            traceId = UUID.randomUUID().toString();
        }

        // Bind traceId to thread-local MDC context
        MDC.put(TRACE_ID_KEY, traceId);

        try {
            chain.doFilter(request, response);
        } finally {
            MDC.remove(TRACE_ID_KEY); // Clean context to prevent thread leak
        }
    }
}

How does Spring Boot handle JSON parsing? Show how to customize the ObjectMapper programmatically.

Jackson Integration: Spring Boot auto-configures the Jackson library's ObjectMapper instance to serialize and deserialize HTTP request/response payloads.

Programmatic Customization: Customize serialization behavior by defining a Jackson2ObjectMapperBuilder bean or registering custom serializer modules.

Common Settings: Common customizations include formatting dates (Java 8 Date/Time API), ignoring null properties, or converting CamelCase keys to SnakeCase.

Custom Deserializers: Use custom deserializers to validate incoming payload constraints or scrub raw input data before it hits the application layers.

JacksonCustomizerConfig.java 
import com.fasterxml.jackson.annotation.JsonInclude;
import com.fasterxml.jackson.databind.ObjectMapper;
import com.fasterxml.jackson.databind.SerializationFeature;
import com.fasterxml.jackson.datatype.jsr310.JavaTimeModule;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Bean;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Configuration;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Primary;

@Configuration
public class JacksonCustomizerConfig {

    @Bean
    @Primary
    public ObjectMapper customObjectMapper() {
        ObjectMapper mapper = new ObjectMapper();
        
        // 1. Support Java 8 Date/Time types
        mapper.registerModule(new JavaTimeModule());
        mapper.disable(SerializationFeature.WRITE_DATES_AS_TIMESTAMPS);
        
        // 2. Exclude null fields from JSON output payload to save bandwidth
        mapper.setSerializationInclusion(JsonInclude.Include.NON_NULL);
        
        // 3. Prevent failure on unknown properties in JSON payload
        mapper.configure(com.fasterxml.jackson.databind.DeserializationFeature.FAIL_ON_UNKNOWN_PROPERTIES, false);
        
        return mapper;
    }
}

Explain Mock annotations in Spring Boot: Compare @Mock vs @MockBean, and @Spy vs @SpyBean.

@Mock: Provided by Mockito. It creates a mock instance of a class for plain unit tests. It does not interact with the Spring context, keeping tests fast.

@MockBean: Provided by Spring Boot. It creates a mock and registers it inside the Spring ApplicationContext, replacing any existing bean of that type for integration testing.

@Spy: Creates a partial mock where real methods are called unless explicitly stubbed, used in standard Mockito test suites.

@SpyBean: Wraps an existing Spring context bean in a spy wrapper, allowing you to track method invocations or stub selective methods during integration tests.

SpringIntegrationTest.java 
import org.junit.jupiter.api.Test;
import org.springframework.beans.factory.annotation.Autowired;
import org.springframework.boot.test.autoconfigure.web.servlet.WebMvcTest;
import org.springframework.boot.test.mock.mockito.MockBean;
import org.springframework.test.web.servlet.MockMvc;
import static org.mockito.Mockito.when;
import static org.springframework.test.web.servlet.request.MockMvcRequestBuilders.get;
import static org.springframework.test.web.servlet.result.MockMvcResultMatchers.status;

@WebMvcTest(UserController.class)
public class SpringIntegrationTest {

    @Autowired
    private MockMvc mockMvc;

    // Registers mock instance in ApplicationContext, replacing the real bean
    @MockBean
    private UserService userService;

    @Test
    public void testGetUserEndpoint() throws Exception {
        // Stub mock behavior
        when(userService.getUserName(1L)).thenReturn("Birat");

        mockMvc.perform(get("/users/1"))
               .andExpect(status().isOk());
    }
}

// Controller under test
class UserController {
    private final UserService service;
    public UserController(UserService service) { this.service = service; }
}
interface UserService { String getUserName(Long id); }

Explain global exception handling in Spring Boot. How do you implement @ControllerAdvice and custom error pages?

Spring MVC provides centralized exception handling to separate error-handling boilerplate from business controllers.

  • @ControllerAdvice / @RestControllerAdvice: Intercepts exceptions thrown by any `@Controller` or `@RestController` in the application.
  • @ExceptionHandler: Placed on advice methods, defining which exception class to handle. The method returns standard HTTP response payloads (`ResponseEntity`) and custom status codes.
  • Custom Error Pages: If an exception escapes controllers and bubbles up to the servlet container, Spring Boot's `BasicErrorController` handles it. By default, it routes to `/error`. You can customize this by creating an HTML file at `/src/main/resources/templates/error/404.html` or `/src/main/resources/public/error/5xx.html`. Spring Boot automatically maps error codes to these specific static or dynamic templates.

GlobalExceptionHandler.java 
import org.springframework.http.HttpStatus;
import org.springframework.http.ResponseEntity;
import org.springframework.web.bind.annotation.ExceptionHandler;
import org.springframework.web.bind.annotation.RestControllerAdvice;
import java.time.LocalDateTime;

// Custom Error DTO
record ErrorResponse(String message, HttpStatus status, LocalDateTime timestamp) {}

// Custom Business Exception
class ResourceNotFoundException extends RuntimeException {
    public ResourceNotFoundException(String message) { super(message); }
}

@RestControllerAdvice
public class GlobalExceptionHandler {

    @ExceptionHandler(ResourceNotFoundException.class)
    public ResponseEntity<ErrorResponse> handleNotFound(ResourceNotFoundException ex) {
        ErrorResponse err = new ErrorResponse(ex.getMessage(), HttpStatus.NOT_FOUND, LocalDateTime.now());
        return new ResponseEntity<>(err, HttpStatus.NOT_FOUND);
    }

    @ExceptionHandler(Exception.class)
    public ResponseEntity<ErrorResponse> handleGeneralException(Exception ex) {
        ErrorResponse err = new ErrorResponse("An internal server error occurred.", HttpStatus.INTERNAL_SERVER_ERROR, LocalDateTime.now());
        return new ResponseEntity<>(err, HttpStatus.INTERNAL_SERVER_ERROR);
    }
}

How does Spring Boot auto-configuration and starters work internally? Provide a complete custom starter auto-configuration.

Spring Boot Starters are dependency aggregators. Adding `spring-boot-starter-web` imports Tomcat, Jackson, validation, and Spring MVC libraries under a single dependency block, ensuring version compatibility.

Auto-Configuration Internals: At startup, Spring Boot scans files named `META-INF/spring/org.springframework.boot.autoconfigure.AutoConfiguration.imports` (Spring Boot 3.x) or `META-INF/spring.factories` (older versions) located inside starter jars on the classpath. This file contains a list of fully-qualified `@Configuration` class names. The container processes these configurations conditionally using `@Conditional` annotations:

  • @ConditionalOnClass: Activate only if specific classes are present on the classpath.
  • @ConditionalOnMissingBean: Register a bean only if the user hasn't defined their own custom bean override.
  • @ConditionalOnProperty: Activate only if a configuration property matches expected values.

CustomTracerAutoConfiguration.java 
import org.springframework.boot.autoconfigure.AutoConfiguration;
import org.springframework.boot.autoconfigure.condition.ConditionalOnClass;
import org.springframework.boot.autoconfigure.condition.ConditionalOnMissingBean;
import org.springframework.boot.autoconfigure.condition.ConditionalOnProperty;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Bean;

// Mock custom tracing library bean
class Tracer {
    private final String prefix;
    public Tracer(String prefix) { this.prefix = prefix; }
    public void trace(String message) { System.out.println(prefix + " -> " + message); }
}

@AutoConfiguration
@ConditionalOnClass(Tracer.class)
@ConditionalOnProperty(prefix = "custom.tracer", name = "enabled", havingValue = "true", matchIfMissing = true)
public class CustomTracerAutoConfiguration {

    @Bean
    @ConditionalOnMissingBean
    public Tracer defaultTracer() {
        return new Tracer("[DEFAULT-APP-TRACE]");
    }
}

How to handle circular dependencies?

Core Answer: In response to the query about how to handle circular dependencies, Avoid via constructor injection. Use @Lazy or setter injection as last resort.

Production Standards: For Spring Boot modules utilizing HandleCircularDependenciesService, enforce proper layering (Controller, Service, Repository), leverage dependency injection (IoC), and separate profiles configuration.

Observability & Monitoring: Track bean creation steps, request handler latencies, and transaction boundary status for HandleCircularDependenciesService using Spring Boot Actuator and SLF4J logs.

Performance Guidelines: Streamline resource consumption for HandleCircularDependenciesService by utilizing lazy bean loading, minimizing database connection times, and configuring connection pool limits.

HandleCircularDependenciesService.java 
package com.valore.enterprise;

import org.slf4j.Logger;
import org.slf4j.LoggerFactory;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;

@Component

How can you customize the default error pages in a Spring Boot application?

Core Answer: In response to the query about customize the default error pages in a spring boot application, Configure Spring Boot initializers, autoconfiguration properties, or exceptions handling related to error page to bootstrap services cleanly and handle runtime errors.

Production Standards: For Spring Boot modules utilizing CustomErrorController, enforce proper layering (Controller, Service, Repository), leverage dependency injection (IoC), and separate profiles configuration.

Observability & Monitoring: Track bean creation steps, request handler latencies, and transaction boundary status for CustomErrorController using Spring Boot Actuator and SLF4J logs.

CustomErrorController.java 
import org.springframework.boot.web.servlet.error.ErrorController;
import org.springframework.web.bind.annotation.RequestMapping;
import org.springframework.web.bind.annotation.RestController;
import jakarta.servlet.http.HttpServletRequest;

@RestController
public class CustomErrorController implements ErrorController {
    @RequestMapping("/error")
    public String handleError(HttpServletRequest request) {
        Integer statusCode = (Integer) request.getAttribute("jakarta.servlet.error.status_code");
        return "Internal Error: " + statusCode;
    }
}

AsyncService.java 
import org.springframework.scheduling.annotation.Async;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Service;
import java.util.concurrent.CompletableFuture;

@Service
public class AsyncService {
    @Async // Executes task asynchronously on task executor thread pool
    public CompletableFuture<String> processAsyncTask() {
        return CompletableFuture.completedFuture("Task Completed");
    }
}

Describe the purpose and usage of the @Async annotation in Spring Boot.

AsyncService.java 
import org.springframework.scheduling.annotation.Async;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Service;
import java.util.concurrent.CompletableFuture;

@Service
public class AsyncService {
    @Async // Executes task asynchronously on task executor thread pool
    public CompletableFuture<String> processAsyncTask() {
        return CompletableFuture.completedFuture("Task Completed");
    }
}

How can you implement request and response logging in a Spring Boot application?

Core Answer: In response to the query about implement request and response logging in a spring boot application, Configure Spring Boot initializers, autoconfiguration properties, or exceptions handling related to request and response logging to bootstrap services cleanly and handle runtime errors.

Production Standards: For Spring Boot modules utilizing RequestLoggingFilter, enforce proper layering (Controller, Service, Repository), leverage dependency injection (IoC), and separate profiles configuration.

Observability & Monitoring: Track bean creation steps, request handler latencies, and transaction boundary status for RequestLoggingFilter using Spring Boot Actuator and SLF4J logs.

Performance Guidelines: Streamline resource consumption for RequestLoggingFilter by utilizing lazy bean loading, minimizing database connection times, and configuring connection pool limits.

RequestLoggingFilter.java 
import jakarta.servlet.*;
import jakarta.servlet.http.HttpServletRequest;
import org.springframework.web.util.ContentCachingRequestWrapper;
import java.io.IOException;

public class RequestLoggingFilter implements Filter {
    @Override
    public void doFilter(ServletRequest req, ServletResponse res, FilterChain chain) throws IOException, ServletException {
        ContentCachingRequestWrapper wrapped = new ContentCachingRequestWrapper((HttpServletRequest) req);
        chain.doFilter(wrapped, res); // Cache request body payload securely
        System.out.println("Request: " + new String(wrapped.getContentAsByteArray()));
    }
}

AutoConfigDemo.java 
import org.springframework.boot.autoconfigure.condition.ConditionalOnClass;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Bean;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Configuration;

@Configuration
@ConditionalOnClass(name = "com.zaxxer.hikari.HikariDataSource")
public class AutoConfigDemo {
    @Bean
    public String databaseConnection() { return "Connected"; }
}

How can you implement logging in a Spring Boot application?

Core Answer: In response to the query about implement logging in a spring boot application, Configure Spring Boot initializers, autoconfiguration properties, or exceptions handling related to logging to bootstrap services cleanly and handle runtime errors.

Production Standards: For Spring Boot modules utilizing logback-spring, enforce proper layering (Controller, Service, Repository), leverage dependency injection (IoC), and separate profiles configuration.

Observability & Monitoring: Track bean creation steps, request handler latencies, and transaction boundary status for logback-spring using Spring Boot Actuator and SLF4J logs.

Performance Guidelines: Streamline resource consumption for logback-spring by utilizing lazy bean loading, minimizing database connection times, and configuring connection pool limits.

logback-spring.xml 
<configuration>
    <appender name="CONSOLE" class="ch.qos.logback.core.ConsoleAppender">
        <encoder>
            <pattern>%d{yyyy-MM-dd HH:mm:ss} [%thread] %-5level %logger{36} - %msg%n</pattern>
        </encoder>
    </appender>
    <root level="INFO">
        <appender-ref ref="CONSOLE"/>
    </root>
</configuration>

import org.slf4j.LoggerFactory;
import org.springframework.web.bind.annotation.RestControllerAdvice;
import org.springframework.web.bind.annotation.ExceptionHandler;

@RestControllerAdvice
public class ErrorLoggingHandler {
    private static final Logger log = LoggerFactory.getLogger(ErrorLoggingHandler.class);

    @ExceptionHandler(Exception.class)
    public void logException(Exception ex) {
        log.error("Fatal transaction failed: {}", ex.getMessage(), ex);
    }
}

logback-spring-prod.xml 
<configuration>
    <!-- Configure Logback to output structured logs to local file -->
    <appender name="FILE" class="ch.qos.logback.core.FileAppender">
        <file>/var/log/valore/app.log</file>
        <encoder>
            <pattern>%d{yyyy-MM-dd HH:mm:ss} [%thread] %-5level - %msg%n</pattern>
        </encoder>
    </appender>
    <root level="ERROR"><appender-ref ref="FILE"/></root>
</configuration>

comperable vs compitable

Comparable vs Comparator: In Java, Comparable and Comparator are interfaces used to define object sorting behaviour. Comparable is implemented by the class of the objects to be sorted, defining their natural ordering via the single method compareTo(T o). Comparator is implemented as a separate class (or lambda) to define custom ordering via the method compare(T o1, T o2), allowing multiple sorting strategies for the same class.

Production Standards: When implementing Comparable, ensure that compareTo is strictly consistent with equals. For custom sorting, leverage Java 8+ static builder methods on the Comparator interface (e.g. Comparator.comparing(), thenComparing()) to write readable, null-safe comparisons.

Observability & Monitoring: Monitor sorting operations inside batch processors using CPU time metrics. Set up alerts if sorting time exceeds SLA bounds, which often signals an accidental fallback to O(N^2) comparison behavior on large datasets.

Performance Guidelines: Keep comparison methods extremely lightweight and free of database calls or object allocations. Cache composite comparison keys when sorting millions of objects to reduce evaluation overhead.

ComparableComparatorDemo.java 
import java.util.ArrayList;
import java.util.Collections;
import java.util.Comparator;
import java.util.List;

// 1. Natural Ordering using Comparable
class Employee implements Comparable<Employee> {
    private final int id;
    private final String name;
    private final double salary;

    public Employee(int id, String name, double salary) {
        this.id = id;
        this.name = name;
        this.salary = salary;
    }

    public String getName() { return name; }
    public double getSalary() { return salary; }

    @Override
    public int compareTo(Employee other) {
        // Natural order: sort by ID ascending
        return Integer.compare(this.id, other.id);
    }

    @Override
    public String toString() {
        return String.format("Employee[ID=%d, Name=%s, Salary=%.1f]", id, name, salary);
    }
}

public class ComparableComparatorDemo {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        List<Employee> list = new ArrayList<>();
        list.add(new Employee(103, "Alice", 95000));
        list.add(new Employee(101, "Bob", 120000));
        list.add(new Employee(102, "Charlie", 85000));

        // Sort using Comparable (natural order by ID)
        Collections.sort(list);
        System.out.println("Sorted by ID: " + list);

        // 2. Custom Ordering using Comparator (sort by Salary descending, then Name)
        Comparator<Employee> salaryThenName = Comparator
            .comparingDouble(Employee::getSalary).reversed()
            .thenComparing(Employee::getName);

        list.sort(salaryThenName);
        System.out.println("Sorted by Salary (desc): " + list);
    }
}

if million of data transection need in rest api , one good practice to that.

High-Volume Transaction Architectures: Processing millions of transactions via REST APIs requires shifting from synchronous blocking designs to asynchronous ingestion models. The standard industry best practice is Queue-Based Load Leveling: the API endpoint validates the incoming payload structure, immediately writes the raw request to a distributed message broker (such as Apache Kafka), and returns a 202 Accepted response containing a job tracking URL. Worker thread pools then process, normalize, and persist the records asynchronously, shielding database resources from sudden traffic spikes.

Production Standards: Enforce rate-limiting at the API Gateway level. Implement client-side batching and utilize bulk-insertion API operations (e.g. batch JDBC updates) to write blocks of records (e.g. 1000 at a time) rather than executing individual SQL inserts.

Observability & Monitoring: Track Kafka consumer lag, end-to-end ingestion latency, error/retry rates, and database thread pool exhaustion metrics using Prometheus and Grafana dashboards.

Performance Guidelines: Use non-blocking WebFlux architectures to minimize thread overhead. Optimize JVM garbage collection parameters (e.g., G1GC or ZGC) to handle transient objects without long stop-the-world pauses.

BulkTransactionProcessor.java 
import org.springframework.beans.factory.annotation.Autowired;
import org.springframework.jdbc.core.JdbcTemplate;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Service;
import org.springframework.transaction.annotation.Transactional;
import java.sql.PreparedStatement;
import java.util.List;

record TransactionRecord(Long userId, double amount, String reference) {}

@Service
public class BulkTransactionProcessor {
    @Autowired
    private JdbcTemplate jdbcTemplate;

    // Use JDBC batch updates for high-throughput bulk insertions
    @Transactional
    public void processBulkTransactions(List<TransactionRecord> records) {
        String sql = "INSERT INTO user_transactions (user_id, amount, reference, created_at) VALUES (?, ?, ?, NOW())";

        // Batch execution groups statements to reduce round-trips
        jdbcTemplate.batchUpdate(sql,
            records,
            1000, // Batch size limit
            (PreparedStatement ps, TransactionRecord record) -> {
                ps.setLong(1, record.userId());
                ps.setDouble(2, record.amount());
                ps.setString(3, record.reference());
            }
        );
    }
}
Asynchronous Streams

Spring WebFlux & Reactive

Reactive programming principles, Mono and Flux, WebClient vs RestTemplate, R2DBC database access, backpressure handling, and Netty event loop non-blocking database scheduler bypass.

How do you stream data non-blockingly using WebClient and CompletableFuture in Spring WebFlux?

Reactive Streams: WebFlux handles streaming using reactive types (Mono, Flux) mapped to the text/event-stream media type.

Producers: Stream items asynchronously using reactive pipelines, preventing worker thread blocks.

Receivers: WebClient consumes streams non-blockingly using bodyToFlux(Data.class), processing elements as they arrive.

CompletableFuture Bridge: To bridge blocking CompletableFuture logic, use Mono.fromFuture() or Flux.create() to adapt async operations into reactive streams.

ReactiveStreamController.java 
import org.springframework.http.MediaType;
import org.springframework.web.bind.annotation.GetMapping;
import org.springframework.web.bind.annotation.RestController;
import org.springframework.web.reactive.function.client.WebClient;
import reactor.core.publisher.Flux;
import reactor.core.publisher.Mono;
import java.time.Duration;
import java.util.concurrent.CompletableFuture;

@RestController
public class ReactiveStreamController {

    // 1. Producer Endpoint - Streams data as Server-Sent Events (SSE)
    @GetMapping(value = "/stream/events", produces = MediaType.TEXT_EVENT_STREAM_VALUE)
    public Flux<String> streamEvents() {
        return Flux.interval(Duration.ofSeconds(1))
                   .map(seq -> "Event Sequence: " + seq);
    }

    // 2. Bridge async Future to Reactive Mono
    public Mono<String> bridgeFuture() {
        CompletableFuture<String> future = CompletableFuture.supplyAsync(() -> "Async Data");
        return Mono.fromFuture(future); // Safe non-blocking bridge
    }

    // 3. Receiver Service - Consumes SSE stream using WebClient
    public void consumeStream() {
        WebClient client = WebClient.create("http://localhost:8080");
        
        Flux<String> eventStream = client.get()
            .uri("/stream/events")
            .accept(MediaType.TEXT_EVENT_STREAM)
            .retrieve()
            .bodyToFlux(String.class);

        eventStream.subscribe(
            data -> System.out.println("Received: " + data),
            err -> System.err.println("Stream error: " + err),
            () -> System.out.println("Stream completed.")
        );
    }
}

Explain Reactive Streams backpressure. How does Spring WebFlux handle it, and what are Mono and Flux?

Backpressure is the mechanism in Reactive Streams that allows a **Consumer** to signal to a **Publisher** how much data it can process at any given moment. This prevents a fast publisher from overwhelming a slow consumer, avoiding memory exhaustion and out-of-memory errors.

In WebFlux, Reactor handles backpressure automatically through TCP flow control. If Netty's outbound write buffers are full, the consumer slows down its demand requests (`Subscription.request(n)`), signaling the upstream publisher to slow down or buffer data.

Mono vs Flux
  • Mono<T>: A Reactive Streams publisher that emits **0 or 1** element, then completes. Useful for single entities, updates, or void results.
  • Flux<T>: A Reactive Streams publisher that emits **0 to N** elements, then completes. Useful for streaming datasets, real-time events, or chunked outputs.
ReactiveController.java 
import org.springframework.web.bind.annotation.GetMapping;
import org.springframework.web.bind.annotation.RestController;
import reactor.core.publisher.Flux;
import reactor.core.publisher.Mono;
import java.time.Duration;

@RestController
public class ReactiveController {

    @GetMapping("/api/v1/ping")
    public Mono<String> ping() {
        return Mono.just("Pong"); // Emits 1 item
    }

    @GetMapping(value = "/api/v1/event-stream", produces = "text/event-stream")
    public Flux<Long> getEventStream() {
        // Emit count increments every 1 second indefinitely
        return Flux.interval(Duration.ofSeconds(1));
    }
}

How do you handle blocking database calls (e.g. JDBC) inside a Spring WebFlux application without starving the Netty Event Loop?

Spring WebFlux is built on a non-blocking reactive event-loop execution model (typically running on Netty). The Netty event loop runs on a very small number of threads (matching CPU cores). If a thread blocks inside the event loop (e.g., executing a synchronous JDBC query, calling a slow blocking REST API, or doing heavy cryptography), **the entire application freezes** because no other incoming requests can be polled.

Scheduler-Bypass Strategy:

To safely invoke blocking library code, isolate the execution using publishOn() to offload execution onto a dedicated thread pool (Schedulers.boundedElastic()). This keeps the main Netty Event Loop threads free to process non-blocking network I/O.

ReactiveService.java 
import reactor.core.publisher.Mono;
import reactor.core.scheduler.Schedulers;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Service;

@Service
public class ReactiveService {

    // Simulates legacy blocking repository call
    private String fetchFromBlockingDb(String id) {
        try { Thread.sleep(500); } catch (InterruptedException e) {}
        return "Data-from-DB-" + id;
    }

    public Mono<String> getReactiveData(String id) {
        return Mono.fromCallable(() -> fetchFromBlockingDb(id))
            // Offload the blocking DB call to boundedElastic scheduler pool
            .subscribeOn(Schedulers.boundedElastic()) 
            .map(data -> data + " | Processed on Non-blocking Pipeline");
    }
}

Endpoints & Access

REST APIs & Security

RESTful controllers, JWT with Spring Security, OAuth2 protocols, Role-Based Access Control, CORS, rate limiting (Bucket4j/Redis), and processing millions of transactions synchronously/asynchronously.

If millions of data transactions need processing via a REST API, what are the best design practices?

Processing millions of records synchronously in a single HTTP request is an anti-pattern. It leads to timeouts, high memory usage, and blocks the client. Here are the correct production-grade approaches:

  1. Asynchronous Processing & Job Status Polling: The API accepts the payload, validates the schema, publishes the job to a message queue or database, and returns `202 Accepted` immediately with a status tracking URL. The client polls this URL (`/api/v1/jobs/{jobId}`) to check progress.
  2. Chunked Batch Processing (Spring Batch): Break the large load into manageable chunks (e.g., 500-1000 records). Read chunk -> Process/Validate -> Write. Use database-level bulk operations (like `EntityManager.flush()` and `clear()` to prevent memory exhaustion).
  3. Streaming with Kafka: Instead of a single large payload, stream records individually to a Kafka topic. A group of consumer services processes these records asynchronously at their own rate, providing natural backpressure and scaling.

BulkProcessingPattern.java 
import jakarta.persistence.EntityManager;
import org.springframework.transaction.annotation.Transactional;
import java.util.List;

public class BulkProcessingPattern {
    private final EntityManager entityManager;

    public BulkProcessingPattern(EntityManager em) { this.entityManager = em; }

    @Transactional
    public void executeBatchInsert(List<Object> largeCollection) {
        int batchSize = 500;
        for (int i = 0; i < largeCollection.size(); i++) {
            entityManager.persist(largeCollection.get(i));
            
            // Periodically flush updates to the DB and clear memory
            if (i > 0 && i % batchSize == 0) {
                entityManager.flush(); // Sends sql updates to database
                entityManager.clear(); // Evicts entity cache to prevent OutOfMemoryError
            }
        }
    }
}

How do you handle distributed payment processing idempotency safely in a Spring REST Controller? Provide a thread-safe Redis-based solution.

Idempotency ensures that making the same API request multiple times produces the exact same outcome as a single request, preventing duplicate charges or duplicate database records. Clients must submit a unique header like `Idempotency-Key` (typically a UUID).

Our solution utilizes Redis to create an atomic **distributed lock** with a Time-To-Live (TTL). When a request arrives:

  1. Check if the key exists in Redis. If yes, reject the request as duplicate or return the cached response.
  2. If no, use Redis `SETNX` (Set if Not Exists) to atomically acquire the lock with a TTL (e.g., 10 minutes) and process the payment.
  3. Store the final transaction response in Redis under the idempotency key, allowing subsequent duplicate requests to return the cached response immediately.

IdempotencyInterceptor.java 
import org.springframework.data.redis.core.StringRedisTemplate;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;
import org.springframework.web.servlet.HandlerInterceptor;
import jakarta.servlet.http.HttpServletRequest;
import jakarta.servlet.http.HttpServletResponse;
import java.time.Duration;

@Component
public class IdempotencyInterceptor implements HandlerInterceptor {

    private final StringRedisTemplate redisTemplate;

    public IdempotencyInterceptor(StringRedisTemplate redisTemplate) {
        this.redisTemplate = redisTemplate;
    }

    @Override
    public boolean preHandle(HttpServletRequest req, HttpServletResponse res, Object handler) throws Exception {
        String key = req.getHeader("Idempotency-Key");
        if (key == null || key.isBlank()) {
            res.sendError(400, "Missing Idempotency-Key header.");
            return false;
        }

        // Redis SETNX: Set key only if it doesn't exist, with 10-minute expiration
        String redisKey = "idempotency:" + key;
        Boolean isLockAcquired = redisTemplate.opsForValue()
            .setIfAbsent(redisKey, "PROCESSING", Duration.ofMinutes(10));

        if (Boolean.FALSE.equals(isLockAcquired)) {
            String status = redisTemplate.opsForValue().get(redisKey);
            if ("PROCESSING".equals(status)) {
                res.sendError(409, "Concurrent duplicate request in progress. Retry shortly.");
            } else {
                res.setStatus(200); // Return cached output
                res.getWriter().write(status); 
            }
            return false;
        }
        return true; // Lock acquired safely, proceed to Controller
    }
}

How do you secure REST endpoints?

Securing enterprise REST services involves integrating authentication, token validation, and authorization filters:

  • JWT (JSON Web Token): An open standard (RFC 7519) that defines a compact, self-contained way to securely transmit information between parties as a JSON object. Signed using a shared secret (HMAC) or public/private key pair (RSA/ECDSA). Components: **Header** (alg, type), **Payload** (claims: sub, roles, exp), and **Signature** (verifies origin and integrity).
  • OAuth 2.0: A delegation protocol. Client applications request an access token from an **Authorization Server** (e.g., Keycloak, Okta) on behalf of a user. The REST endpoint acts as a **Resource Server**, validating the token signature against the Authorization Server's public keys (JWKS endpoint).
  • RBAC (Role-Based Access Control): Restricting resource access based on user roles (extracted from JWT claims). Managed using Spring Security configuration or method-level annotations (`@PreAuthorize("hasRole('ADMIN')")`).

SecurityConfig.java 
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Bean;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Configuration;
import org.springframework.security.config.annotation.web.builders.HttpSecurity;
import org.springframework.security.web.SecurityFilterChain;

@Configuration
public class SecurityConfig {
    @Bean
    public SecurityFilterChain filterChain(HttpSecurity http) throws Exception {
        http.csrf(csrf -> csrf.disable())
            .authorizeHttpRequests(auth -> auth
                .requestMatchers("/api/v1/auth/**").permitAll()
                .anyRequest().authenticated()
            );
        return http.build();
    }
}

What is rate limiting and how do you implement it?

RateLimitFilter.java 
import io.github.bucket4j.Bandwidth;
import io.github.bucket4j.Bucket;
import io.github.bucket4j.Refill;
import java.time.Duration;

public class RateLimitFilter {
    private final Bucket bucket = Bucket.builder()
            .addLimit(Bandwidth.classic(10, Refill.intervally(10, Duration.ofMinutes(1))))
            .build();

    public boolean allowRequest() { return bucket.tryConsume(1); }
}

JwtTokenProvider.java 
import io.jsonwebtoken.Jwts;
import io.jsonwebtoken.SignatureAlgorithm;
import java.util.Date;

public class JwtTokenProvider {
    public String buildToken(String user) {
        return Jwts.builder()
                   .setSubject(user)
                   .setExpiration(new Date(System.currentTimeMillis() + 8640000))
                   .signWith(SignatureAlgorithm.HS512, "secretkey")
                   .compact();
    }
}

What Is Spring Security?

Core Answer: In response to the query about spring security, Implement enterprise-grade Spring configurations, dependency injections, or aspects related to spring to support modular architecture and cross-cutting concerns.

SpringSecurityService.java 
package com.valore.enterprise;

import org.slf4j.Logger;
import org.slf4j.LoggerFactory;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;

@Component
public class SpringSecurityService {
    private static final Logger log = LoggerFactory.getLogger(SpringSecurityService.class);

    public void processTask() {
        log.info("Executing custom enterprise business logic flow...");
    }

Distributed Systems

Microservices & Cloud

Inter-service communication (Feign, gRPC), API Gateways, distributed tracing (Sleuth/OpenTelemetry), Saga pattern (Orchestration vs Choreography), Outbox pattern, eventual consistency, and AWS Lambda with GraalVM.

What are the core Dockerfile keywords used to containerize Spring Boot microservices?

FROM & WORKDIR: FROM sets the base parent image (e.g., Eclipse Temurin JRE). WORKDIR configures the working directory for subsequent instructions.

COPY & ENV: COPY transfers local files (like build artifacts) into the container's filesystem. ENV defines system environment variables available at runtime.

EXPOSE: Expose documents the port the application listens on inside the container.

ENTRYPOINT & CMD: ENTRYPOINT configures the container to run as an executable. CMD defines default arguments for the entrypoint.

Dockerfile 
# Multistage Build to keep the final image size minimal and secure
# Stage 1: Build the Maven application
FROM maven:3.8.5-openjdk-17-slim AS build
WORKDIR /app
COPY pom.xml .
COPY src ./src
RUN mvn clean package -DskipTests

# Stage 2: Create lightweight execution container
FROM eclipse-temurin:17-jre-alpine
WORKDIR /workspace
COPY --from=build /app/target/*.jar app.jar

ENV PORT=8080
EXPOSE 8080

ENTRYPOINT ["java", "-jar", "app.jar"]

How do you implement centralized logging in microservices? Show how to trace a request across service boundaries.

Centralized Logs: Aggregate stdout/stderr logs from all containers into a centralized store like ELK (Elasticsearch, Logstash, Kibana) or Splunk.

Distributed Tracing: Trace IDs track requests across service boundaries. Span IDs represent work within a specific microservice.

Context Propagation: OpenTelemetry or Micrometer Tracing propagate trace context in HTTP headers (e.g. W3C Trace Context) to maintain end-to-end trace visibility.

MDC Logs: Mapped Diagnostic Context (MDC) binds trace metadata to the active execution thread log context, printing it automatically in log messages.

TraceContextLoggingFilter.java 
import org.slf4j.Logger;
import org.slf4j.LoggerFactory;
import org.slf4j.MDC;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Component;
import org.springframework.web.client.RestTemplate;
import org.springframework.http.HttpHeaders;
import org.springframework.http.HttpEntity;

@Component
public class TraceContextLoggingFilter {
    private static final Logger log = LoggerFactory.getLogger(TraceContextLoggingFilter.class);

    public void processServiceChain(RestTemplate restTemplate) {
        String activeTraceId = MDC.get("traceId");
        log.info("Sending request to payment microservice with traceId: {}", activeTraceId);

        // Propagate the Trace ID downstream in the outgoing request headers
        HttpHeaders headers = new HttpHeaders();
        headers.set("X-Correlation-ID", activeTraceId);
        HttpEntity<String> entity = new HttpEntity<>(headers);

        String response = restTemplate.postForObject("http://PAYMENT-SERVICE/pay", entity, String.class);
        log.info("Received downstream payment status response: {}", response);
    }
}

Compare Vertical vs Horizontal Scaling. How do you scale microservices dynamically to handle sudden spikes in API requests?

Vertical Scaling: Increases the capacity (CPU, RAM, Disk) of a single server. It is simple but limited by hardware limits.

Horizontal Scaling: Adds more server instances. It supports linear scaling and provides high availability.

Kubernetes Autoscaling: Microservices scale horizontally using Kubernetes Horizontal Pod Autoscaler (HPA), which monitors CPU/Memory utilization to adjust pod counts.

Handling Spikes: To handle traffic spikes, pair auto-scaling with ingress rate-limiting (Redis token bucket) and message queuing (Kafka) to buffer traffic.

KubernetesAutoscalerConfig.yaml 
apiVersion: autoscaling/v2
kind: HorizontalPodAutoscaler
metadata:
  name: order-service-hpa
  namespace: valore-prod
spec:
  scaleTargetRef:
    apiVersion: apps/v1
    kind: Deployment
    name: order-service
  minReplicas: 3
  maxReplicas: 30
  metrics:
  - type: Resource
    resource:
      name: cpu
      target:
        type: Utilization
        averageUtilization: 75 # Scale up when average CPU utilization hits 75%
  - type: Resource
    resource:
      name: memory
      target:
        type: Utilization
        averageValue: 500Mi

Explain the Circuit Breaker pattern with a real microservice example. How do you declare fallback methods in Spring Boot?

Circuit Breaker Pattern: Resilience4j intercepts calls to external services. If failures exceed a set threshold, the breaker transitions to the OPEN state.

Fallback Execution: In the OPEN state, requests fail-fast to local fallback methods, preventing cascading failures across the system.

Half-Open Testing: The breaker enters HALF-OPEN after a cooldown period, executing test calls to determine if the downstream service has recovered.

Declarative Policies: Combine `@CircuitBreaker` and `@Retry` to implement transparent, policy-based fault tolerance.

OrderProcessingService.java 
import io.github.resilience4j.circuitbreaker.annotation.CircuitBreaker;
import io.github.resilience4j.retry.annotation.Retry;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Service;
import org.springframework.web.client.RestTemplate;

@Service
public class OrderProcessingService {

    private final RestTemplate restTemplate;
    public OrderProcessingService(RestTemplate restTemplate) { this.restTemplate = restTemplate; }

    @CircuitBreaker(name = "paymentService", fallbackMethod = "paymentFallback")
    @Retry(name = "paymentService")
    public String executePayment(String orderId, double amount) {
        String url = "http://PAYMENT-SERVICE/pay?order=" + orderId + "&amount=" + amount;
        return restTemplate.postForObject(url, null, String.class);
    }

    // Fallback executes immediately when Circuit Breaker is OPEN or error occurs
    public String paymentFallback(String orderId, double amount, Throwable t) {
        System.out.println("Payment service down! Running fallback for: " + orderId);
        return "PAYMENT_PENDING_FALLBACK_REASON: " + t.getMessage();
    }
}

Why do microservice architectures use API Gateways and Service Registries? Compare load balancing types.

API Gateway: Acts as the single entry point. It manages routing, authentication, SSL termination, and rate limiting.

Service Registry: (e.g. Eureka) dynamically keeps track of the changing IP addresses and port numbers of active microservice instances.

Load Balancer: Distributes incoming traffic across healthy service instances, preventing single-node bottlenecks.

Client-Side Load Balancing: (e.g., Spring Cloud LoadBalancer) allows the client to fetch instance lists and load balance directly, reducing gateway hops.

GatewayRoutingConfig.yml 
spring:
  cloud:
    gateway:
      discovery:
        locator:
          enabled: true # Fetch route mappings dynamically from Eureka
      routes:
        - id: order-service-route
          uri: lb://ORDER-SERVICE # Load balance across ORDER-SERVICE instances
          predicates:
            - Path=/api/orders/**
          filters:
            - StripPrefix=1
            - name: RequestRateLimiter
              args:
                redis-rate-limiter.replenishRate: 50
                redis-rate-limiter.burstCapacity: 100
                key-resolver: "#{@userKeyResolver}"

eureka:
  client:
    service-url:
      defaultZone: http://localhost:8761/eureka/

How do you optimize Spring Boot for serverless (AWS Lambda) using GraalVM? Detail Native Image optimization and Runtime Hints.

Classic Spring Boot applications have a high startup time (warm-up) and a large memory footprint, making them inefficient for serverless environments where cold starts incur latency and cost.

GraalVM Native Image compiles Java bytecode ahead-of-time (AOT) into a standalone native executable tailored for a specific target OS. This results in **millisecond-level startup times** and a drastically reduced memory footprint, making it ideal for AWS Lambda.

The Challenge: Reflection & Dynamic Proxies: GraalVM performs closed-world analysis, assuming all reachable classes and resources must be known at compile-time. Dynamic features like Java Reflection, resource bundle loading, serialization, and dynamic proxies (extensively used in Spring) are not automatically visible to the compiler and will fail at runtime.

To resolve this, we configure RuntimeHintsRegistrar to declare dynamic classes, resource paths, and reflection bindings, allowing GraalVM to compile them into the binary safely.

LambdaRuntimeHint.java 
import org.springframework.aot.hint.RuntimeHints;
import org.springframework.aot.hint.RuntimeHintsRegistrar;
import org.springframework.aot.hint.TypeReference;

// Declare reflection runtime hint requirements for GraalVM AOT Compilation
public class LambdaRuntimeHint implements RuntimeHintsRegistrar {
    @Override
    public void registerHints(RuntimeHints hints, ClassLoader classLoader) {
        // Register custom DTO for reflection mapping (e.g., Jackson JSON serialization)
        hints.reflection().registerType(
            TypeReference.of("com.serverless.dto.UserRequest"),
            builder -> builder.withMembers(
                org.springframework.aot.hint.MemberCategory.INVOKE_PUBLIC_CONSTRUCTORS,
                org.springframework.aot.hint.MemberCategory.INVOKE_PUBLIC_METHODS
            )
        );

        // Register classpath resource bundle
        hints.resources().registerPattern("configs/custom-schema.json");
    }
}

Compare Saga Orchestration vs Choreography. Explain how the Outbox Pattern ensures eventual consistency.

In a microservice architecture, managing transactions across multiple databases requires eventual consistency strategies:

  • Saga Pattern: Decomposes a distributed transaction into a sequence of local database transactions. If one step fails, the Saga triggers compensatory transactions (rollback steps) in reverse order.
    • **Choreography**: Services publish and subscribe to events without a central coordinator. High decoupling, but difficult to track flow complexity.
    • **Orchestration**: A central coordinator service directs the workflow, telling each service when to execute its transaction. Simpler monitoring, but introduces a single point of failure and coupling.
  • Transactional Outbox Pattern: Prevents data inconsistency when updating a database and publishing message events (like Kafka) in the same action. Writing directly to the message broker can fail due to network drops, leaving the DB updated but the message lost. Instead, write both the domain entity update and an outbox event into the **same database transaction** (in an `OUTBOX` table). An independent background poll process (or a Change Data Capture tool like Debezium) reads the `OUTBOX` table and reliably publishes messages to the broker.

OutboxPublisher.java 
import org.springframework.stereotype.Service;
import org.springframework.transaction.annotation.Transactional;
import java.util.UUID;

@Service
public class OutboxPublisher {
    private final OutboxRepository outboxRepo;
    private final OrderRepository orderRepo;

    @Transactional
    public void createOrder(Order order) {
        orderRepo.save(order); // 1. Save business entity
        OutboxEvent event = new OutboxEvent(
            UUID.randomUUID().toString(),
            "OrderCreated",
            order.getId().toString()
        );
        outboxRepo.save(event); // 2. Write event to outbox table (same transaction)
    }
}

How do you implement retry, fallback, and circuit breaker?

In distributed systems, failures of downstream microservices are inevitable. To prevent a cascade of failures, apply resilience patterns:

  • Circuit Breaker: Monitors error rates on calls. If errors exceed a threshold (e.g., 50% failures), the circuit **trips open**, failing subsequent calls instantly without hitting the downstream service. After a cooldown period, it enters a **half-open** state to check if the downstream service has recovered.
  • Retry: Retries failed calls with exponential backoff and jitter to handle transient network blips.
  • Fallback: Provides a default, degraded response (e.g., cached data or static catalog) when the downstream call fails or is short-circuited.

ProductService.java 
import io.github.resilience4j.circuitbreaker.annotation.CircuitBreaker;
import io.github.resilience4j.retry.annotation.Retry;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Service;
import org.springframework.web.client.RestTemplate;
import java.util.Collections;
import java.util.List;

@Service
public class ProductService {
    private final RestTemplate restTemplate;

    public ProductService(RestTemplate restTemplate) {
        this.restTemplate = restTemplate;
    }

    @CircuitBreaker(name = "catalogService", fallbackMethod = "fallbackProducts")
    @Retry(name = "catalogService")
    public List<String> getProducts() {
        // Direct blocking API call to downstream microservice
        return restTemplate.getForObject("http://catalog-service/api/v1/products", List.class);
    }

    // Fallback method must have matching signature + exception parameter
    public List<String> fallbackProducts(Throwable exception) {
        System.err.println("Circuit open or catalog-service failed. Triggering fallback.");
        return Collections.singletonList("Static Catalog Item (Fallback)");
    }
}

TraceContextFilter.java 
import jakarta.servlet.*;
import org.slf4j.MDC;
import java.io.IOException;
import java.util.UUID;

public class TraceContextFilter implements Filter {
    @Override
    public void doFilter(ServletRequest req, ServletResponse res, FilterChain chain) 
            throws IOException, ServletException {
        MDC.put("traceId", UUID.randomUUID().toString()); // Set trace logging variable
        try {
            chain.doFilter(req, res);
        } finally {
            MDC.clear();
        }
    }
}

public class SagaCoordinator {

            chargeUser();
            deductInventory();
