how to maintain db consistency and concueenrency when multiple rest api call same end point. how to microservices handle

Database Concurrency & Consistency: Single-service concurrency is managed using Optimistic Locking (adding a version check column: UPDATE ... WHERE id = ? AND version = :currentVersion, throwing OptimisticLockingFailureException on collision) or Pessimistic Locking (executing SELECT ... FOR UPDATE at the DB level, blocking concurrent updates). Microservices handle consistency across services using the Saga Pattern (Orchestrated or Choreographed compensations) combined with the Transactional Outbox Pattern to publish events atomically with local database mutations.

Production Standards: Implement idempotency validation on endpoints via an Idempotency-Key header. For microservices, use event-driven messaging with transactional outbox tables and Kafka to guarantee eventual consistency and avoid partial failures.

Observability & Monitoring: Monitor transaction lock waits, deadlock frequencies, and database rollback ratios in APM dashboard. Track Saga orchestration failures and compensatory transaction execution logs.

Performance Guidelines: Prefer Optimistic Locking for high-read/low-write scenarios to keep operations non-blocking. Minimize transaction scopes and execution times, and never invoke external HTTP calls within transactional blocks.

DistributedConsistencyService.java 
import org.springframework.stereotype.Service;
import org.springframework.transaction.annotation.Transactional;
import jakarta.persistence.*;
import java.util.UUID;

@Entity
class UserAccount {
    @Id
    private Long id;
    private double balance;
    
    @Version // Optimistic Lock Version
    private int version;

    public void deduct(double amount) {
        if (balance < amount) throw new IllegalArgumentException("Insufficient funds");
        this.balance -= amount;
    }
}

@Service
public class DistributedConsistencyService {
    @PersistenceContext
    private EntityManager entityManager;

    // 1. Single Service Optimistic Concurrency Control
    @Transactional
    public void updateAccountBalance(Long id, double amount, String idempotencyKey) {
        // Enforce Idempotency using database constraints or Redis
        if (isDuplicateRequest(idempotencyKey)) {
            throw new IllegalStateException("Duplicate transaction request");
        }

        UserAccount account = entityManager.find(UserAccount.class, id);
        account.deduct(amount); // Version auto-checked and incremented on commit
    }

    // 2. Microservice transactional outbox publisher pattern (Saga step)
    @Transactional
    public void executeSagaStep(Long id, double amount, String sagaId) {
        UserAccount account = entityManager.find(UserAccount.class, id);
        account.deduct(amount);

        // Write event to the same DB atomically
        OutboxEvent event = new OutboxEvent(
            UUID.randomUUID().toString(),
            sagaId,
            "ACCOUNT_DEDUCTED",
            String.format("{\"accountId\":%d,\"amount\";%.2f}", id, amount)
        );
        entityManager.persist(event);
    }

    private boolean isDuplicateRequest(String key) {
        // Checking mechanism stub
        return false;
    }
}

@Entity
class OutboxEvent {
    @Id private String id;
    private String sagaId;
    private String eventType;
    private String payload;
    
    public OutboxEvent(String id, String sagaId, String eventType, String payload) {
        this.id = id; this.sagaId = sagaId; this.eventType = eventType; this.payload = payload;
    }
}
Event-Driven Messaging

Apache Kafka

Kafka components, partitions, consumer groups, fault tolerance, message ordering, offset management, exactly-once semantics, ZooKeeper vs KRaft, retry topics, and Dead Letter Topics (DLT).

Explain Kafka Producer, Consumer, Offset, and Group Rebalancing. How do you handle message spikes?

Producer & Consumer: Producers write records to partition keys, which determines the target partition log. Consumers subscribe to topics, reading records sequentially.

Offsets: Offsets record the sequential read progress of each consumer, which is stored in the internal `__consumer_offsets` topic.

Consumer Groups: Multiple consumers in a consumer group read from separate partitions in parallel, which is managed by a coordinator.

Handling Spikes: Scale partitions to distribute load. Designing topics: set partitions >= consumer count, use specific partition keys to prevent hot spots, and configure replication factors.

KafkaProducerConsumerDemo.java 
import org.apache.kafka.clients.producer.KafkaProducer;
import org.apache.kafka.clients.producer.ProducerRecord;
import org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer.KafkaConsumer;
import org.apache.kafka.clients.consumer.ConsumerRecords;
import java.time.Duration;
import java.util.Collections;
import java.util.Properties;

public class KafkaProducerConsumerDemo {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        Properties config = new Properties();
        config.put("bootstrap.servers", "localhost:9092");
        config.put("key.serializer", "org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringSerializer");
        config.put("value.serializer", "org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringSerializer");

        // 1. Publishing an event using a partition key
        KafkaProducer<String, String> producer = new KafkaProducer<>(config);
        ProducerRecord<String, String> record = new ProducerRecord<>("orders-topic", "customer-101", "OrderPlacedEventData");
        producer.send(record);
        producer.close();

        // 2. Subscribing to consume events
        Properties consumerConfig = new Properties();
        consumerConfig.put("bootstrap.servers", "localhost:9092");
        consumerConfig.put("group.id", "order-processing-group");
        consumerConfig.put("key.deserializer", "org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer");
        consumerConfig.put("value.deserializer", "org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringDeserializer");

        KafkaConsumer<String, String> consumer = new KafkaConsumer<>(consumerConfig);
        consumer.subscribe(Collections.singletonList("orders-topic"));
        
        // Continuous poll loop
        ConsumerRecords<String, String> records = consumer.poll(Duration.ofMillis(100));
        System.out.println("Fetched message count: " + records.count());
        consumer.close();
    }
}

Write a robust, non-blocking Kafka consumer with Spring Boot that handles processing failures using retry topics and routes poison pills to a DLT.

In high-throughput event processing, a single malformed message (poison pill) or a transient processing failure must not halt the consumer. Blocking retries (`Thread.sleep()`) are problematic because if processing takes too long, the broker assumes the consumer is dead, triggering partition rebalancing and halting the pipeline.

The standard solution is **Non-blocking Retries & Dead Letter Topics (DLT)**:

  1. If a message fails, catch the exception and immediately publish the record to a retry topic (e.g., `orders-retry-10s`) and commit the offset on the main topic.
  2. An independent consumer processes messages from the retry topic after a delay.
  3. If retries are exhausted, the recovery coordinator publishes the message to a Dead Letter Topic (`orders-dlt`) for manual debugging, allowing the main pipeline to continue processing.

KafkaConsumerConfig.java 
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Bean;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Configuration;
import org.springframework.kafka.annotation.KafkaListener;
import org.springframework.kafka.core.KafkaTemplate;
import org.springframework.kafka.listener.DeadLetterPublishingRecoverer;
import org.springframework.kafka.listener.DefaultErrorHandler;
import org.springframework.kafka.support.serializer.DeserializationException;
import org.springframework.util.backoff.FixedBackOff;

@Configuration
public class KafkaConsumerConfig {

    // Define retry/DLT error handler
    @Bean
    public DefaultErrorHandler errorHandler(KafkaTemplate<Object, Object> template) {
        // Route failed records to DLT topic (e.g. topic-name.DLT)
        DeadLetterPublishingRecoverer recoverer = new DeadLetterPublishingRecoverer(template);
        
        // Configure retry: 3 attempts, 1-second delay
        DefaultErrorHandler errorHandler = new DefaultErrorHandler(recoverer, new FixedBackOff(1000L, 2));
        
        // Do NOT retry deserialization failures (poison pills) — send directly to DLT!
        errorHandler.addNotRetryableException(DeserializationException.class);
        
        return errorHandler;
    }

    @KafkaListener(id = "orderGroup", topics = "orders-main-topic")
    public void listen(String orderEvent) {
        System.out.println("Processing event: " + orderEvent);
        if (orderEvent.contains("FAIL")) {
            throw new RuntimeException("Simulated processing exception.");
        }
    }
}

What are the core components of Kafka? Explain partition structuring, consumer groups, offset tracking, and ZooKeeper vs KRaft.

Apache Kafka is a distributed event streaming platform built on an append-only transaction log:

  • Brokers & Clusters: Brokers are nodes that receive, write, and serve data records. A cluster is a group of brokers cooperating for high availability.
  • Topics & Partitions: A Topic is a logical category of events. Topics are split into **Partitions** (physical commit logs). Partitions allow Kafka to scale by distributing data across multiple brokers, enabling concurrent writes and reads.
  • Consumer Groups & Rebalancing: A group of consumers that cooperate to read messages from a set of partitions. Each partition is assigned to **exactly one** consumer in the group, ensuring message ordering per partition. If a consumer joins or leaves, Kafka triggers a **rebalance** to reassign partitions.
  • Offset Tracking: Consumers track their reading progress using offsets. Offsets are committed back to a special internal Kafka topic: `__consumer_offsets`.
  • ZooKeeper vs KRaft: Historically, Kafka used ZooKeeper to manage cluster metadata, leader elections, and configuration. In modern Kafka, **KRaft** (Kafka Raft Metadata Mode) replaces ZooKeeper. KRaft stores metadata directly inside Kafka partitions managed by controller nodes, simplifying architecture, speeding up partition leader elections, and improving scalability.

KafkaConfig.java 
import org.apache.kafka.clients.producer.ProducerConfig;
import org.apache.kafka.common.serialization.StringSerializer;
import java.util.Properties;

public class KafkaConfig {
    public Properties producerConfigs() {
        Properties props = new Properties();
        props.put(ProducerConfig.BOOTSTRAP_SERVERS_CONFIG, "localhost:9092");
        props.put(ProducerConfig.KEY_SERIALIZER_CLASS_CONFIG, StringSerializer.class.getName());
        props.put(ProducerConfig.VALUE_SERIALIZER_CLASS_CONFIG, StringSerializer.class.getName());
        props.put(ProducerConfig.ACKS_CONFIG, "all"); // High reliability guarantees
        return props;
    }
}

Persistence Layer

Databases & Transactions

SQL joins, index types (B-Tree, Clustered/Non-clustered), ACID properties, isolation levels vs database propagation, optimistic vs pessimistic locking, and resolving the JPA N+1 problem using JOIN FETCH.

What are the fastest strategies for microservices to communicate with databases under high concurrency?

Connection Pooling: Use HikariCP to reuse database connections, minimizing thread allocation latency.

Caching: Implement a read-through cache using Redis or Caffeine to prevent redundant select queries from reaching the database.

Read-Write Splitting: Route write transactions to the primary database node, and balance read requests across read-replicas.

Reactive Access: Use R2DBC for non-blocking reactive persistence, preventing database driver threads from blocking the Netty event loops.

HighLoadDbConfiguration.java 
import com.zaxxer.hikari.HikariConfig;
import com.zaxxer.hikari.HikariDataSource;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Bean;
import org.springframework.context.annotation.Configuration;
import javax.sql.DataSource;

@Configuration
public class HighLoadDbConfiguration {

    @Bean
    public DataSource hikariDataSource() {
        HikariConfig config = new HikariConfig();
        config.setJdbcUrl("jdbc:postgresql://localhost:5432/valoredb");
        config.setUsername("dbuser");
        config.setPassword("securepwd");
        
        // Tuning for high concurrency
        config.setMaximumPoolSize(40);
        config.setMinimumIdle(10);
        config.setIdleTimeout(30000);
        config.setConnectionTimeout(2000);
        config.setLeakDetectionThreshold(2000); // Detect connection leaks
        
        // Cache settings optimization for Postgres
        config.addDataSourceProperty("cachePrepStmts", "true");
        config.addDataSourceProperty("prepStmtCacheSize", "250");
        config.addDataSourceProperty("prepStmtCacheSqlLimit", "2048");

        return new HikariDataSource(config);
    }
}

How do you write custom queries in Spring Boot? Compare JpaRepository method derivation, JPQL, and Native SQL Queries.

Method Derivation: Generates SQL automatically from the method signature (e.g. findByEmailAndStatus). It is convenient but unsuitable for complex joins.

JPQL (Java Persistence Query Language): Queries Java entities and properties rather than raw database tables, maintaining database engine independence.

Native SQL Queries: Execute raw SQL directly against the database engine, bypassing entity mapping rules. This is useful for database-specific optimizations.

Selection Guide: Use derived queries for basic CRUD operations, JPQL for general entity joins, and native queries for batch statements or using database-specific extensions.

UserRepository.java 
import org.springframework.data.jpa.repository.JpaRepository;
import org.springframework.data.jpa.repository.Query;
import org.springframework.data.repository.query.Param;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Repository;
import java.util.List;

@Repository
public interface UserRepository extends JpaRepository<NamespaceMigrationDemo, Long> {

    // 1. Method Derivation (Automatic SQL generation)
    List<NamespaceMigrationDemo> findByUsername(String username);

    // 2. Custom JPQL Query (Targets Entity classes and properties)
    @Query("SELECT u FROM NamespaceMigrationDemo u WHERE u.username LIKE %:suffix%")
    List<NamespaceMigrationDemo> searchByUsernameSuffix(@Param("suffix") String suffix);

    // 3. Native SQL Query (Executes raw database-specific statements)
    @Query(value = "SELECT * FROM users WHERE id > :minId LIMIT 10", nativeQuery = true)
    List<NamespaceMigrationDemo> fetchUserLimit(@Param("minId") Long minId);
}

What are SQL ACID properties? Compare Stored Procedures vs Functions in relational database design.

ACID Properties: Atomicity (all operations commit or all rollback), Consistency (data state complies with schema constraints), Isolation (concurrent transactions execute independently), and Durability (committed changes survive system failures).

Stored Procedures: Precompiled blocks of SQL statements executed on the database server. They can execute transactional commits/rollbacks, return multiple result sets, and perform raw DML operations.

Functions: Must return exactly one value. They cannot execute transactional commands or modify database state, but can be embedded inside queries.

Best Practice: Use stored procedures for complex maintenance jobs and batch data operations, and functions for calculated expressions (like custom string formatting).

ProcVsFunctionDemo.sql 
-- 1. Stored Procedure - supports DML and transactions
CREATE OR REPLACE PROCEDURE transfer_balance(
    sender_id INT,
    receiver_id INT,
    amount DECIMAL
)
LANGUAGE plpgsql AS $$
BEGIN
    UPDATE accounts SET balance = balance - amount WHERE id = sender_id;
    UPDATE accounts SET balance = balance + amount WHERE id = receiver_id;
    
    COMMIT; -- Commit transaction inside procedure
END;
$$;

-- 2. User Defined Function - returns value, no transaction control
CREATE OR REPLACE FUNCTION get_interest_gain(balance DECIMAL, rate DECIMAL)
RETURNS DECIMAL AS $$
BEGIN
    RETURN balance * (rate / 100);
END;
$$ LANGUAGE plpgsql;

Discuss SQL Joins, Order By, Group By, Indexes, and Database Sharding strategies.

SQL Joins: Combine data rows from multiple tables using matching column keys. Inner Join returns matching rows; Left Join preserves all rows from the left table.

Order By & Group By: ORDER BY sorts query results. GROUP BY aggregates data rows based on matching columns (e.g. sum sales by product category).

Indexing: B-Tree indexes speed up query lookups by creating sorted search tables, but add storage overhead and slow down DML write speeds.

Database Sharding: A horizontal partitioning strategy that distributes a single logical database across multiple physical servers to support high-throughput scaling.

AdvancedQueriesAndIndex.sql 
-- 1. Advanced Join with Group By and Order By aggregation
SELECT c.name, COUNT(e.id) as employee_count, AVG(e.salary) as avg_salary
FROM Company c
INNER JOIN Employee e ON c.id = e.company_id
WHERE e.is_active = true
GROUP BY c.name
HAVING COUNT(e.id) > 5
ORDER BY avg_salary DESC;

-- 2. Create index to speed up join and group by lookup
CREATE INDEX idx_employee_company_active ON Employee(company_id, is_active);

-- 3. Sharding partition mapping concept (Horizontal division based on key)
-- CREATE TABLE customer_usa PARTITION OF customer FOR VALUES IN ('USA');

What is Lazy Loading in Hibernate? How do you prevent and resolve LazyInitializationException?

Lazy Loading: Defers database fetching of associated collections or entities until they are explicitly accessed, replacing associations with runtime bytecode proxies.

LazyInitializationException: Occurs when a transaction has closed (terminating the active Session context), and code subsequently attempts to access an uninitialized lazy property.

Resolution Strategies: Resolve this by: (1) Using @EntityGraph to fetch dependencies selectively, (2) using JOIN FETCH inside JPQL, or (3) maintaining the transaction boundary using @Transactional.

Best Practice: Avoid setting FetchType.EAGER globally, as it triggers performance degradation and creates JPA N+1 query execution loops.

LazyInitializationFix.java 
import org.springframework.data.jpa.repository.JpaRepository;
import org.springframework.data.jpa.repository.Query;
import org.springframework.data.repository.query.Param;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Repository;
import java.util.Optional;

@Repository
interface CompanyRepository extends JpaRepository<Company, Long> {

    // Resolves LazyInitializationException and N+1 query loops using JOIN FETCH
    @Query("SELECT c FROM Company c JOIN FETCH c.employees WHERE c.id = :id")
    Optional<Company> findCompanyWithEmployees(@Param("id") Long id);
}

/*
// Example Usage in Transaction Context:
@Service
class CompanyService {
    private final CompanyRepository repo;
    public CompanyService(CompanyRepository repo) { this.repo = repo; }

    @Transactional(readOnly = true)
    public void printEmployees(Long id) {
        Company c = repo.findCompanyWithEmployees(id)
            .orElseThrow(() -> new RuntimeException("Not found"));
        // Safe: Employees collection is fully populated in a single query
        c.getEmployees().forEach(System.out::println);
    }
}
*/

How do you map @OneToMany and @ManyToOne bidirectional relationships? Compare Hibernate Session vs SessionFactory.

Relationship Mapping: In bidirectional mappings, define @ManyToOne on the child entity (representing the foreign key) and @OneToMany(mappedBy = "...") on the parent.

Owner Side: Always use mappedBy to define the relationship owner, preventing Hibernate from generating an unnecessary third join table.

SessionFactory: A heavyweight, thread-safe factory bean created during startup that caches metadata and handles L2 cache configuration.

Session: A lightweight, non-thread-safe connection wrapper representing the L1 cache. It is short-lived and tied to a single database transaction.

BidirectionalMappingDemo.java 
import jakarta.persistence.*;
import java.util.ArrayList;
import java.util.List;

@Entity
class Company {
    @Id @GeneratedValue(strategy = GenerationType.IDENTITY)
    private Long id;

    // mappedBy points to company field in Employee entity
    @OneToMany(mappedBy = "company", cascade = CascadeType.ALL, orphanRemoval = true)
    private List<Employee> employees = new ArrayList<>();

    public void addEmployee(Employee emp) {
        employees.add(emp);
        emp.setCompany(this); // Sync bidirectional association link
    }
}

@Entity
class Employee {
    @Id @GeneratedValue(strategy = GenerationType.IDENTITY)
    private Long id;
    
    private String name;

    @ManyToOne(fetch = FetchType.LAZY)
    @JoinColumn(name = "company_id") // Foreign key column
    private Company company;

    public void setCompany(Company company) { this.company = company; }
}

Explain Soft Delete vs Hard Delete. How do you implement automated soft deletion in Spring Data JPA?

Hard Delete: Executes a standard DELETE FROM query, permanently removing rows from the database storage blocks.

Soft Delete: Updates a status flag (e.g. is_deleted = true), hiding the row from queries while preserving historical data for audits and restores.

Hibernate Integration: In JPA, soft delete is automated using Hibernate's @SQLDelete (replaces delete operations with an update statement) and @Where (adds filter clauses automatically to select queries).

Referential Integrity: Use soft deletes to maintain referential integrity in relational tables while preventing data loss, but note that custom indexes must handle the deleted rows to prevent index bloat.

SoftDeleteEntity.java 
import jakarta.persistence.*;
import org.hibernate.annotations.SQLDelete;
import org.hibernate.annotations.Where;

@Entity
@Table(name = "products")
// Intercept JPA delete entity commands and turn them into Updates
@SQLDelete(sql = "UPDATE products SET is_deleted = true WHERE id = ?")
// Automatically filter out deleted products from select queries
@Where(clause = "is_deleted = false")
public class SoftDeleteEntity {
    @Id
    @GeneratedValue(strategy = GenerationType.IDENTITY)
    private Long id;

    private String name;
    
    private double price;

    private boolean isDeleted = false; // Soft delete tracking flag

    public SoftDeleteEntity() {}

    public Long getId() { return id; }
    public String getName() { return name; }
    public void setDeleted(boolean deleted) { this.isDeleted = deleted; }
}

How do you maintain database consistency under high concurrency? Explain Optimistic vs Pessimistic locking.

When multiple concurrent API calls update the same database records simultaneously, conflicts occur (lost updates, dirty reads). We manage concurrency using locking mechanisms:

  • Optimistic Locking: Assumes conflicts are rare. Every record includes a `@Version` attribute (integer or timestamp). When updating, the SQL includes a check: `UPDATE table SET ... version = version + 1 WHERE id = ? AND version = :currentVersion`. If another transaction updated the record first, the version mismatch causes the update to fail, throwing an OptimisticLockingFailureException. This approach is non-blocking and highly scalable.
  • Pessimistic Locking: Assumes conflicts are frequent. It locks rows physically at the database level using `SELECT ... FOR UPDATE` (Pessimistic Write). Concurrent readers/writers must block and wait until the transaction completes and releases the lock. This prevents conflicts but reduces concurrency and can lead to database deadlocks.

OptimisticLockingExample.java 
import jakarta.persistence.*;

@Entity
public class Account {
    @Id
    @GeneratedValue(strategy = GenerationType.IDENTITY)
    private Long id;

    private double balance;

    @Version // Automatically managed by JPA for Optimistic Locking
    private int version;

    public void withdraw(double amount) {
        if (balance < amount) throw new RuntimeException("Insufficient funds.");
        this.balance -= amount;
    }
}

// Service Layer Catching Lock Conflicts
@Service
public class AccountService {
    @Autowired private AccountRepository repo;

    @Transactional
    public void processWithdrawal(Long id, double amount) {
        try {
            Account account = repo.findById(id).orElseThrow();
            account.withdraw(amount);
            repo.save(account);
        } catch (ObjectOptimisticLockingFailureException e) {
            // Optimistic lock failed! Handle by retrying the operation
            System.err.println("Conflict detected. Retrying transaction...");
        }
    }
}

What is the JPA N+1 problem and how do you diagnose and fix it? Provide a JPA repository JOIN FETCH solution.

The N+1 problem occurs when Hibernate loads a parent entity, then issues N additional SQL queries to load child associations for each of the N parent entities (typically caused by lazy loading or using standard `findAll()`).

For example, if you fetch a list of 100 `Author` records and iterate over them to print their `Book` objects:

  • 1 query is executed to fetch the 100 `Author` objects.
  • 100 additional queries are executed to fetch the `Book` objects for each author individually.
This results in 101 database roundtrips, degrading application performance.

Resolution (JOIN FETCH):

Instruct JPA to load both parent and child associations in a single database roundtrip using a SQL **INNER JOIN FETCH** or **LEFT JOIN FETCH** in a custom JPQL query.

AuthorRepository.java 
import org.springframework.data.jpa.repository.JpaRepository;
import org.springframework.data.jpa.repository.Query;
import org.springframework.data.repository.query.Param;
import org.springframework.stereotype.Repository;
import java.util.List;

@Repository
public interface AuthorRepository extends JpaRepository<Author, Long> {

    // 🛑 Avoid: findAll() -> triggers N+1 queries when accessing nested lazy associations

    // ✅ Resolve via JOIN FETCH: Loads Author and Books in 1 SQL query!
    @Query("SELECT a FROM Author a LEFT JOIN FETCH a.books")
    List<Author> findAllWithBooks();

    @Query("SELECT a FROM Author a LEFT JOIN FETCH a.books WHERE a.id = :id")
    Author findByIdWithBooks(@Param("id") Long id);
}

Compare SQL Joins (INNER, LEFT, RIGHT, FULL) and Indexing internals (B-Tree, Clustered vs Non-Clustered).

Understanding database retrieval mechanics is essential for query optimization:

  • SQL Joins:
    • **INNER JOIN**: Returns rows only when there is a match in both tables.
    • **LEFT JOIN**: Returns all rows from the left table, plus matched rows from the right table. Unmatched right-side values yield `NULL`.
    • **RIGHT JOIN**: Returns all rows from the right table, plus matched rows from the left table.
    • **FULL JOIN**: Returns all rows when there is a match in either the left or right table.
  • Indexing Internals (B-Tree): An index is a balanced tree data structure (B-Tree / B+ Tree) that avoids O(N) table scans, enabling O(log N) searches. Nodes contain keys and child pointers, guiding queries down to the correct data pages.
  • Clustered vs Non-Clustered Indexes:
    • **Clustered Index**: Determines the physical storage order of rows in the table. There can be **exactly one** clustered index per table (typically the Primary Key). The leaf nodes of a clustered index contain the actual data rows.
    • **Non-Clustered Index**: A separate structure containing index keys and pointers (row locators/logical addresses) back to the actual data rows in the clustered index. A table can have multiple non-clustered indexes.

DatabaseQuery.sql 
-- Inner and Left Join Query Example
SELECT emp.id, emp.name, dept.name AS department
FROM Employee emp
LEFT JOIN Department dept ON emp.department_id = dept.id;

-- Index Creation optimization
CREATE INDEX idx_employee_dept ON Employee(department_id);

-- LEFT JOIN: all users, even those without orders
SELECT u.name, o.id FROM users u LEFT JOIN orders o ON u.id = o.user_id;

How to prevent SQL Injection in Java?

SecureQuery.java 
import java.sql.*;

public class SecureQuery {
    public void executeSecure(Connection conn, String user) throws Exception {
        String query = "SELECT * FROM users WHERE username = ?";
        try (PreparedStatement pstmt = conn.prepareStatement(query)) {
            pstmt.setString(1, user); // Parameters automatically escaped
            pstmt.executeQuery();
        }
    }
}

Logic & Optimization

Algorithms & Data Structures

ArrayList vs LinkedList (reversing a linked list in-place), HashMap internals (bucket indexing, treeification), binary search, QuickSort, BFS vs DFS, and high-performance TTL LRU cache.

Implement in-place LinkedList reversal, Binary Search, and explain BFS vs DFS graph traversals.

Key algorithmic patterns frequently used in software development:

  • In-Place LinkedList Reversal: Reverse a singly linked list iteratively by maintaining three pointers: `prev`, `curr`, and `next`. Point `curr.next` to `prev`, then move the pointers forward. O(N) time complexity, O(1) auxiliary space complexity.
  • Binary Search: A divide-and-conquer search algorithm on a **sorted array**. Compare the search key with the array's midpoint. If they match, return the index. If the key is smaller, narrow search to the left half; if larger, to the right half. O(log N) search time complexity.
  • BFS vs DFS Graph Traversals:
    • **BFS (Breadth-First Search)**: Explores a graph level-by-level, visiting all neighbor nodes first. Implemented using a **Queue**. Best for finding the shortest path in unweighted graphs.
    • **DFS (Depth-First Search)**: Explores a branch as deep as possible before backtracking. Implemented using recursion or a **Stack**. Best for topological sorting, finding connected components, and resolving dependencies.

LinkedListInPlaceReversal.java 
public class LinkedListInPlaceReversal {
    static class Node {
        int val;
        Node next;
        Node(int val) { this.val = val; }
    }

    public static Node reverse(Node head) {
        Node prev = null;
        Node curr = head;
        Node next = null;

        while (curr != null) {
            next = curr.next; // Store next node
            curr.next = prev; // Reverse current node link
            prev = curr;      // Move prev pointer forward
            curr = next;      // Move curr pointer forward
        }
        return prev; // New head of reversed list
    }
}

Implement a thread-safe, high-performance Least Recently Used (LRU) cache with a Time-To-Live (TTL) expiration mechanism in pure Java.

A classic LRU cache maintains O(1) reads and writes by combining a **HashMap** (for lookup) with a **Doubly Linked List** (to track usage order). When a key is accessed, we move its node to the head of the list. When the cache is full, we evict the node at the tail of the list.

To support a Time-To-Live (TTL) expiration mechanism, we store the creation/write timestamp in each cache node. During read access, we check if the current time exceeds the node's TTL. If expired, we remove the node immediately and return null. We ensure thread safety by wrapping accesses inside a ReentrantReadWriteLock, permitting multiple concurrent readers but isolating writers.

ConcurrentLruCache.java 
import java.util.HashMap;
import java.util.Map;
import java.util.concurrent.locks.ReentrantReadWriteLock;

public class ConcurrentLruCache<K, V> {
    private static class CacheNode<K, V> {
        K key;
        V value;
        long expiryTime;
        CacheNode<K, V> prev, next;
        
        CacheNode(K key, V value, long ttlMs) {
            this.key = key;
            this.value = value;
            this.expiryTime = System.currentTimeMillis() + ttlMs;
        }
    }

    private final int capacity;
    private final Map<K, CacheNode<K, V>> map = new HashMap<>();
    private final CacheNode<K, V> head, tail;
    private final ReentrantReadWriteLock lock = new ReentrantReadWriteLock();

    public ConcurrentLruCache(int capacity) {
        this.capacity = capacity;
        this.head = new CacheNode<>(null, null, 0);
        this.tail = new CacheNode<>(null, null, 0);
        head.next = tail;
        tail.prev = head;
    }

    public V get(K key) {
        lock.readLock().lock();
        try {
            CacheNode<K, V> node = map.get(key);
            if (node == null) return null;
            
            // Check TTL expiration
            if (System.currentTimeMillis() > node.expiryTime) {
                // Upgrade to write lock to evict expired item
                lock.readLock().unlock();
                lock.writeLock().lock();
                try {
                    evictNode(node);
                } finally {
                    lock.writeLock().unlock();
                    lock.readLock().lock(); // Downgrade lock
                }
                return null;
            }
            
            // Move accessed node to head (indicates recently used)
            lock.readLock().unlock();
            lock.writeLock().lock();
            try {
                moveToHead(node);
            } finally {
                lock.writeLock().unlock();
                lock.readLock().lock();
            }
            return node.value;
        } finally {
            lock.readLock().unlock();
        }
    }

    public void put(K key, V value, long ttlMs) {
        lock.writeLock().lock();
        try {
            CacheNode<K, V> existing = map.get(key);
            if (existing != null) {
                existing.value = value;
                existing.expiryTime = System.currentTimeMillis() + ttlMs;
                moveToHead(existing);
                return;
            }

            if (map.size() >= capacity) {
                // Evict least recently used (tail.prev)
                evictNode(tail.prev);
            }

            CacheNode<K, V> newNode = new CacheNode<>(key, value, ttlMs);
            map.put(key, newNode);
            addNodeAtHead(newNode);
        } finally {
            lock.writeLock().unlock();
        }
    }

    private void addNodeAtHead(CacheNode<K, V> node) {
        node.next = head.next;
        node.prev = head;
        head.next.prev = node;
        head.next = node;
    }

    private void removeNode(CacheNode<K, V> node) {
        node.prev.next = node.next;
        node.next.prev = node.prev;
    }

    private void moveToHead(CacheNode<K, V> node) {
        removeNode(node);
        addNodeAtHead(node);
    }

    private void evictNode(CacheNode<K, V> node) {
        removeNode(node);
        map.remove(node.key);
    }
}

Explain HashMap internals, bucket indexing, collision resolution, and Java 8 treeification.

HashMap is built on an array of nodes (buckets): `Node[] table`.

Core Operations:
  1. Bucket Indexing: When you call `put(key, value)`, HashMap calculates the key's hash using `hash = key.hashCode() ^ (hash >>> 16)` (spreads high bits down to reduce collisions). The bucket index is calculated using bitwise AND: `index = hash & (n - 1)` (where `n` is the array length, which is always a power of 2).
  2. Collision Resolution: If two keys map to the same index, they are stored in a linked list at that bucket node (chaining).
  3. Java 8 Treeification: If a linked list at a single bucket grows beyond a threshold (TREEIFY_THRESHOLD = 8) and the total table capacity is at least 64, HashMap converts the linked list into a balanced **Red-Black Tree**. This improves search time from O(N) to O(log N) for collided buckets. If the size shrinks below 6, it untreeifies back to a list.

Write a function to check if a string is a palindrome.

Core Answer: In response to the query about write a function to check if a string is a palindrome., Use two-pointer technique or reverse and compare.

Production Standards: When writing algorithmic logic involving palindrome, specify clear edge cases, enforce input parameter validations, and document the Big-O time and space complexities.

Observability & Monitoring: Track execution times, stack call depth sizes, and heap allocation bottlenecks for palindrome using unit performance test suites.

Performance Guidelines: Optimize search and sort efficiency for palindrome by minimizing nested loops, utilizing hash lookups, and applying memoization caching techniques where applicable.

Palindrome.java 
public class Palindrome {
    public boolean check(String s) {
        int left = 0, right = s.length() - 1;
        while (left < right) {
            if (s.charAt(left) != s.charAt(right)) return false;
            left++; right--;
        }
        return true;
    }
}

Explain binary search algorithm.

Core Answer: In response to the query about binary search algorithm., Efficient O(log n) algorithm on sorted arrays. Divide and conquer.

Production Standards: When writing algorithmic logic involving binary search, specify clear edge cases, enforce input parameter validations, and document the Big-O time and space complexities.

Observability & Monitoring: Track execution times, stack call depth sizes, and heap allocation bottlenecks for binary search using unit performance test suites.

Performance Guidelines: Optimize search and sort efficiency for binary search by minimizing nested loops, utilizing hash lookups, and applying memoization caching techniques where applicable.

BinarySearch.java 
public class BinarySearch {
    public int search(int[] list, int target) {
        int low = 0, high = list.length - 1;
        while (low <= high) {
            int mid = low + (high - low) / 2; // Prevents mid bounds integer overflow
            if (list[mid] == target) return mid;
            if (list[mid] < target) low = mid + 1;
            else high = mid - 1;
        }
        return -1;
    }
}

How does QuickSort work?

Core Answer: In response to the query about quicksort work, Divide around a pivot. Sort partitions recursively. Average O(n log n).

Production Standards: When writing algorithmic logic involving quicksort, specify clear edge cases, enforce input parameter validations, and document the Big-O time and space complexities.

Observability & Monitoring: Track execution times, stack call depth sizes, and heap allocation bottlenecks for quicksort using unit performance test suites.

Performance Guidelines: Optimize search and sort efficiency for quicksort by minimizing nested loops, utilizing hash lookups, and applying memoization caching techniques where applicable.

QuickSort.java 
public class QuickSort {
    public void sort(int[] arr, int low, int high) {
        if (low < high) {
            int p = partition(arr, low, high);
            sort(arr, low, p - 1);
            sort(arr, p + 1, high);
        }
    }
    private int partition(int[] arr, int l, int h) {
        int pivot = arr[h], i = l - 1;
        for (int j = l; j < h; j++) {
            if (arr[j] < pivot) { i++; swap(arr, i, j); }
        }
        swap(arr, i + 1, h); return i + 1;
    }
    private void swap(int[] a, int i, int j) { int t = a[i]; a[i] = a[j]; a[j] = t; }
}

HeapSort.java 
import java.util.PriorityQueue;

public class HeapSort {
    public static void main(String[] args) {
        // PriorityQueue acts as a balanced binary min-heap internally
        PriorityQueue<Integer> minHeap = new PriorityQueue<>();
        minHeap.offer(10); minHeap.offer(5); minHeap.offer(20);
        while (!minHeap.isEmpty()) { System.out.println(minHeap.poll()); }
    }
}

Implement LRU cache.

Core Answer: In response to the query about implement lru cache., Use LinkedHashMap or a combination of HashMap + Doubly Linked List.

Performance Guidelines: Optimize search and sort efficiency for lru by minimizing nested loops, utilizing hash lookups, and applying memoization caching techniques where applicable.

LruCache.java 
import java.util.LinkedHashMap;
import java.util.Map;

public class LruCache<K, V> extends LinkedHashMap<K, V> {
    private final int capacity;
    public LruCache(int cap) {
        super(cap, 0.75f, true); // true sets Access-Order tracking
        this.capacity = cap;
    }
    @Override protected boolean removeEldestEntry(Map.Entry<K, V> eldest) {
        return size() > capacity; // Automatically evicts oldest accessed node
    }
}

What’s a Trie? Use case?

Core Answer: In response to the query about what’s a trie? use case, Tree-like structure for prefix searching (e.g., autocomplete).

TrieTree.java 
import java.util.HashMap;
import java.util.Map;

class TrieNode {
    Map<Character, TrieNode> children = new HashMap<>();
    boolean isWord = false;
}
public class TrieTree {
    private final TrieNode root = new TrieNode();
    public void insert(String word) {
        TrieNode curr = root;
        for (char ch : word.toCharArray()) {
            curr = curr.children.computeIfAbsent(ch, c -> new TrieNode());
        }
        curr.isWord = true;
    }
}

How do you detect a cycle in a graph?

Core Answer: In response to the query about how do you detect a cycle in a graph, DFS with visited set or Union-Find.

Production Standards: When writing algorithmic logic involving cycle, specify clear edge cases, enforce input parameter validations, and document the Big-O time and space complexities.

Observability & Monitoring: Track execution times, stack call depth sizes, and heap allocation bottlenecks for cycle using unit performance test suites.

Performance Guidelines: Optimize search and sort efficiency for cycle by minimizing nested loops, utilizing hash lookups, and applying memoization caching techniques where applicable.

CycleDetection.java 
import java.util.List;

public class CycleDetection {
    public boolean hasCycle(int v, List<List<Integer>> adj) {
        int[] state = new int[v]; // 0: unvisited, 1: visiting, 2: visited
        for (int i = 0; i < v; i++) {
            if (state[i] == 0 && dfs(i, adj, state)) return true;
        }
        return false;
    }
    private boolean dfs(int n, List<List<Integer>> adj, int[] state) {
        state[n] = 1;
        for (int neighbor : adj.get(n)) {
            if (state[neighbor] == 1) return true;
            if (state[neighbor] == 0 && dfs(neighbor, adj, state)) return true;
        }
        state[n] = 2; return false;
    }
}

How do you find the intersection of two arrays?

Core Answer: In response to the query about how do you find the intersection of two arrays, Use Set or HashMap to track common elements.

Production Standards: When writing algorithmic logic involving intersection, specify clear edge cases, enforce input parameter validations, and document the Big-O time and space complexities.

Observability & Monitoring: Track execution times, stack call depth sizes, and heap allocation bottlenecks for intersection using unit performance test suites.

Performance Guidelines: Optimize search and sort efficiency for intersection by minimizing nested loops, utilizing hash lookups, and applying memoization caching techniques where applicable.

ArrayIntersection.java 
import java.util.HashSet;
import java.util.Set;

public class ArrayIntersection {
    public int[] intersect(int[] n1, int[] n2) {
        Set<Integer> set = new HashSet<>();
        for (int val : n1) set.add(val);
        Set<Integer> res = new HashSet<>();
        for (int val : n2) { if (set.contains(val)) res.add(val); }
        return res.stream().mapToInt(Integer::intValue).toArray();
    }
}

FibonacciMemo.java 
public class FibonacciMemo {
    public int fib(int n, int[] memo) {
        if (n <= 1) return n;
        if (memo[n] != 0) return memo[n];
        memo[n] = fib(n - 1, memo) + fib(n - 2, memo);
        return memo[n];
    }
}
Frontend Architecture

React Framework (3 YOE)

React rendering lifecycle, virtual DOM, React hooks (useState, useEffect, useMemo, useCallback), routing, and state management (Zustand stores, Context API, Redux comparisons).

How do you optimize rendering performance in React? Implement an optimized state consumer component with Zustand.

React components re-render whenever their parent re-renders or their state changes. In large applications, unnecessary rendering cycles cause UI lag.

Rendering Optimization Hooks:
  • useMemo: Memoizes expensive computations, executing them only when dependencies change.
  • useCallback: Memoizes function instances, preventing child components from re-rendering due to inline function reference changes.
  • React.memo: A higher-order component that skips rendering a component if its incoming props are unchanged.
State Management: Zustand vs Context API

The Context API causes **all consuming components to re-render** whenever the context value changes, even if a component only consumes a subset of the value. Zustand is a lightweight state management library that solves this. It uses a publish-subscribe model with **selector-based state subscription**, ensuring components re-render only when the specific sliced attribute they select changes.

ZustandDashboardStore.js 
import { create } from 'zustand';

// 1. Create a global Zustand store
export const useDashboardStore = create((set) => ({
    userCount: 150,
    serverStatus: "HEALTHY",
    notifications: [],
    
    incrementUserCount: () => set((state) => ({ userCount: state.userCount + 1 })),
    setServerStatus: (status) => set({ serverStatus: status }),
    addNotification: (msg) => set((state) => ({ 
        notifications: [...state.notifications, { id: Date.now(), msg }] 
    }))
}));

// 2. React Consumer Component with Optimized Selector Subscription
export function UserCounterComponent() {
    // Selector subscription: Component ONLY re-renders when 'userCount' changes!
    const userCount = useDashboardStore((state) => state.userCount);
    const increment = useDashboardStore((state) => state.incrementUserCount);

    console.log("UserCounterComponent rendered!"); // Confirms render isolation

    return (
        <div className="card p-3 border-0 bg-dark text-white rounded">
            <h5>Total Active Users: {userCount}</h5>
            <button className="btn btn-primary btn-sm mt-2" onClick={increment}>
                Add User
            </button>
        </div>
    );
}

Explain the React component lifecycle, virtual DOM reconciliation, and the usage rules of Hooks.

React is built on a declarative programming model with efficient UI updates:

  • Virtual DOM Reconciliation (Fiber): React maintains a lightweight virtual DOM tree in memory. When state changes, React generates a new Virtual DOM tree. The **reconciliation algorithm (Fiber)** compares the new tree with the old tree (diffing) and batches the minimal set of updates required to modify the real DOM, avoiding expensive layout reflows.
  • Rules of Hooks: Hooks (introduced in React 16.8) allow functional components to manage state and lifecycle side effects.
    1. **Only Call Hooks at the Top Level**: Do not call hooks inside loops, conditions, or nested functions. This ensures React executes hooks in the exact same order on every render.
    2. **Only Call Hooks from React Functions**: Call hooks only from React functional components or custom hooks.
  • Core Hooks:
    • `useState`: Manages local component state.
    • `useEffect`: Manages side effects (API calls, subscriptions, DOM mutations). Fires asynchronously after rendering completes.
    • `useRef`: Retains mutable values that do not trigger re-renders, and offers direct access to DOM nodes.

ComponentLifecycle.jsx 
import React, { useState, useEffect } from 'react';

export default function ComponentLifecycle() {
    const [status, setStatus] = useState('Idle');

    useEffect(() => {
        setStatus('Mounted');
        console.log('Component mounted (reconciliation complete)');
        
        return () => {
            console.log('Component unmounted (cleanup hook executed)');
        };
    }, []); // Empty dependency array run-once constraint

    return <div>Component Status: {status}</div>;
}
